Tech
Next Games Raises $6 Million from Investors; Finnish Mobile-Game Developer to Launch Title Based on TV Series 'The Walking Dead'

Juhana Rossi 
617 words
12 March 2014
17:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

HELSINKI Next Games, a Finnish mobile-game developer with ties to Hollywood, has collected a round of funding to launch a pair of new titles, including a game based on AMC Networks Inc.'s hit television series "The Walking Dead."

Next Games said Tuesday it raised $6 million from a group of investors from three continents.

Jari Ovaskainen, a Switzerland-based Finnish investor and early backer of games developer Supercell Oy, was part of a group of investors that included China's IDG Capital Partners.

The funding round marks the first time a Chinese investor has put money into a Finnish mobile-game developer.

AMC, a U.S. television company that aired the show "Breaking Bad," is also funding the venture.

Next Games, which was founded 10 months ago, is looking to replicate the success of Supercell, which created the free-to-download "Hay Day" and "Clash of Clans" games that are packed with upgrades that have to be purchased. Japanese telecommunications company SoftBank Corp. paid $1.5 billion for a 51% stake in Supercell last year.

Next Games's fortunes got a boost last week when it announced a licensing deal with AMC intended for a game related to "The Walking Dead," a post-apocalyptic drama based on a comic book.

The company has spent much of its initial capital developing technical specifications for "free to play" mobile games that can be readily improved and expanded over time so that users are compelled to keep playing and given the incentive to buy upgrades. "For example, when a new location appears in a 'Walking Dead' episode, we could quickly feature it in our game," Next Games Chief Executive Teemu Huuhtanen said in an interview.

The financial backing from AMC "signals that [the network] views our collaboration as a long-term strategic partnership," said Mr. Huuhtanen, who led mergers and acquisitions for Rovio Entertainment Ltd., maker of the Angry Birds franchise, before founding Next Games. Joakim Achrn, one of Next Games's founders, is a games-industry veteran who previously worked as analytics director at Supercell.

Mr. Huuhtanen said "The Walking Dead" game is likely to be launched by the spring of next year.

In addition to "The Walking Dead," Next Games is working on a game code-named "West." Mr. Achrn said it would be an action and strategy game, but declined to disclose further details.

The success of Supercell, Rovio and a number of smaller studios has boosted the Finnish videogame industry, which generated an estimated $1.2 billion in combined revenue in 2013 and is attracting money from marquee international venture-capital firms.

Other investors in Next Games include U.S. venture-capital firms Lowercase Capital LLC and York Ventures LLC, a newcomer that is backed by brothers Alec, Tom and Sam Gores.

International interest could be a positive sign for Finland's startup scene. "Typically, Finnish app vendors have in the past received early funding from Finland, Sweden and the U.K.," said Tero Kuittinen, a mobile-application analyst with Alekstra who is based in New York.

Mr. Kuittinen, a Next Games adviser, said Asian investors' growing interest in Finnish game developers has been stirred in part by the developers' recent strong performance in Japan, which has a reputation as being a tough market to crack for foreign companies.

Corrections & Amplifications

Investors in Next Games, a Finnish mobile-games developer, included IDG Capital Partners, AMC Networks Inc., Lowercase Capital LLC and York Ventures LLC. A previous version of this article incorrectly said the group of investors included Accel Partners.

Tech
King Digital IPO Plans Boosting Hope for London Tech Scene; "Candy Crush" Developer Seeks Valuation up to $7.6 Billion

Amir Mizroch 
734 words
12 March 2014
20:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LONDON With King Digital Entertainment PLC's plans to go public, this financial center can now lay claim to a major player in today's global tech frenzy.

The Anglo-Swedish developer of "Candy Crush Saga" gave a price range for its planned initial public offering Wednesday seeking to raise just over $500 million and a valuation of as much as $7.6 billion on the New York Stock Exchange.

King's provenance isn't a clear-cut matter. It was founded in Sweden, where much of its games-development work still takes place. But its Italian chief executive and much of its administration is based in London.

That presence is boosting hope among entrepreneurs and investors here that London's nascent tech scene is starting to garner some global street cred. Meanwhile, a wave of local tech companies are planning their own listings at home. Just-Eat.co.uk, an online takeaway food service, and online retailer the Hut Group are both planning near-term IPOs on the London Stock Exchange, according to people familiar with the matter.

Seventeen U.K. tech companies floated on the LSE last year, raising $735 million, up from just three companies the year before, according to Dealogic.

Equity financing for tech venture-backed companies in London hit $755 million last year, beating out Berlin, another European capital that is actively recruiting tech talent, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. But that still lags behind the $9.7 billion invested in Silicon Valley and the $2.4 billion invested in New York-based companies.

Despite London's place at the center of the financial world, tech companies have struggled to get attention from established investors here. Entrepreneurs and tech executives blame a cultural gap between the City, as London's financial district is known, and its startup scene.

"The City is generally risk averse and doesn't understand tech and tech startups," says Steve Karmeinsky, a veteran tech entrepreneur and programmer here. But the global splash King has made with its IPO is now emboldening many in the tech sector here.

"The more stories like [King], the better impact and influence it has on the institutional investor market," said Ami Shpiro, founder of Innovation Warehouse, a local accelerator, nestled within a poultry wholesale market at the edge of "Tech City," a once-grungy stretch of East London that has recently lured dozens of startups and tech-centric companies.

Two years ago, Google Inc. opened Campus London, a seven-storey development in which Google provides free workspace for tech entrepreneurs, along with a basement cafeteria and ping-pong tables.

The tech frenzy has seeped into London's booming property market helping jack up rents in some tech-popular districts, and luring some of the city's real estate players. Canary Wharf Group PLC, one of London's biggest commercial-property developers started up and manages Level 39, the 39th floor of a tower overlooking the River Thames at Canary Wharf, one of London's banking districts. Level 39 rents out workspace to finance- and retail-focused tech companies, among other tenants.

With the King IPO, London has established itself as a new European center of excellence for gaming-technology companies, alongside longtime Nordic powers like Sweden and Finland. Mind Candy Ltd., a character and online gaming company best known for "Moshi Monster," is another London-based giant in the field. Founder Michael Acton Smith is a stalwart of the London tech scene.

London is already a center for financial technology, or fintech technology geared toward the back offices of financial services companies. And London's position as a global advertising capital has also fostered an "adtech" scene. Adbrain, for instance, uses artificial intelligence and probability calculations to match people across devices, helping advertisers figure out what type of consumer is using a certain device, and bid on relevant ad space, said Chief Executive Gareth Davies.

"It's a mathematical challenge of tying a lot of data points together in a privacy-compliant way to help marketers understand who their consumers are," he said. The company Tuesday announced $7.5 million funding from Octopus Investments and Notion Capital.

Home Loans Find New Hands --- Mortgage Servicing Shifts to Nonbank Firms, Fueling Concern Among Regulators [CORRECTED]

By Stephanie Armour, Andrew R. Johnson and Rob Copeland 
1,208 words
10 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright  2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Corrections & Amplifications In talking last year about the troubles facing some corners of the mortgage-servicing industry, James Dimon, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.'s chief executive, said, "If I had a choice, I would never be into default servicing again." A March 10, 2014, Money & Investing article about the mortgage-servicing business incorrectly quoted Mr. Dimon as saying "bulk servicing."

Washington's effort to push banks out of the mortgage-servicing business is propelling the handling of customers' loans into companies such as hedge funds and nonbank financial firms.

The shift is fueling concern among federal and state regulators about the level of oversight and capital requirements in the industries now servicing a growing share of these loans.

Banks such as Morgan Stanley, Bank of America Corp., Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Ally Financial Inc., have been selling mortgage-servicing rights to nonbank companies, including Ocwen Financial Corp., and Nationstar Mortgage Holdings Inc., which have doubled their servicing portfolios in the past year.

About $1.03 trillion of mortgage-servicing rights were sold in 2013, with the vast majority going to nonbank firms, said Guy Cecala, publisher and chief executive officer of industry newsletter Inside Mortgage Finance. Among the 30 largest mortgage servicers, nonbank firms held a 17% market share at the end of 2013, up from 9% at the end of 2012 and 6% at the end of 2011.

The business can be lucrative. Servicers typically make money by collecting a fee from the mortgage's owner -- usually a bank or investor -- for handling billing and payment collection. Ten of the largest U.S. mortgage lenders took in $8.23 billion in servicing income in 2013, according to an analysis by Inside Mortgage Finance.

But it has become a liability for lenders, which face new restrictions as Washington tries to siphon risk out of the banking system. Regulators, concerned about banks' exposure to mortgage-related assets and their alleged ham-handedness in handling crisis-era loans of troubled borrowers, have introduced new rules dictating how mortgage servicers interact with borrowers and raising capital costs for servicing loans.

"If I had a choice, I would never be into bulk servicing again," J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. Chief Executive James Dimon said at the company's investor day last month. The bank is working to reduce the size of its servicing business for defaulted mortgages, officials said at the event.

As banks scale back, other firms are stepping in: Structured Portfolio Management, LLC, a hedge fund, recently told investors it plans to get into the mortgage-servicing business by running its own company, said people familiar with the decision. Another hedge fund, Premium Point Investments LP, has started a separate arm to buy whole loans, bundle and sell them and retain the servicing rights even after they are sold.

But regulators have pointed out that new entrants aren't subject to the same oversight and capital rules aimed at helping them weather market stress.

The Financial Stability Oversight Council, a group of top regulators including the Treasury secretary and Federal Reserve chairman, noted the shift in its 2013 annual report, saying "many large depository institutions are reducing their mortgage servicing activity and removing potential risk from their balance sheets by selling servicing rights to non-bank servicers, which are not subject to the same capital requirements as banks."

In an interview, Benjamin Lawsky, superintendent of the New York Department of Financial Services, called the rapid growth of nonbank mortgage servicers "eyebrow raising" given the role the companies play in interacting with homeowners.

Mr. Lawsky attributed the growth of nonbank servicers partly to Washington's implementation of international banking standards that have made it more expensive for banks to hold certain mortgage assets.

"The Basel rules are certainly welcome, but they appear to be having this unintended consequence on some of the banks desiring to shed these" assets, Mr. Lawsky said. "I don't think that's such a bad thing. We're not saying that nonbank servicers can't do this. Our concern as a regulator is capacity."

Some firms already have run into trouble over their handling of customers' mortgages. In December, Ocwen reached a $2.1 billion settlement with the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and state regulators over allegations the company charged unauthorized fees, failed to properly credit borrowers' accounts and other issues. Ocwen didn't admit or deny the allegations.

The New York Department of Financial Services last month halted Ocwen's deal to acquire the rights to service $39 billion of mortgages from Wells Fargo & Co., the nation's largest home-loan servicer, citing concerns about Ocwen's ability to handle more loans given complaints the agency has received from consumers.

The Fed and other bank regulators intended for new capital and other rules to push some servicing business out of banks, which they worried weren't able to adequately assess their exposure to mortgage assets.

The CFPB, a consumer regulator, has established rules for all servicers -- including those owned by nonbank companies -- that prevent servicers from initiating foreclosure in the first 120 days a borrower is delinquent. But nonbank firms generally aren't subject to the types of federal capital requirements and other restrictions that regulators place on banks.

"This is the first clear case of rules restructuring a banking business into a shadow one," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner at Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

Structured Portfolio Management recently told investors it was close to announcing plans to run its own mortgage-banking operating business. In a letter to investors, founder Don Brownstein alluded to "limitations on bank activities, both regulatory and otherwise, [which] are providing a set of opportunities for us."

"This whole business was obliterated by the government, and there's a lot of money in it," said Rael Gorelick, partner at Gorelick Brothers Capital LLC, Charlotte, N.C., which invests in hedge funds and other assets with exposure to U.S. housing markets. Mr. Gorelick said the hedge funds investing in and operating mortgage servicers are incentivized to keep owners in their homes, as the business is most profitable when borrowers continue to make payments and avoid default.

"I can't imagine the government would be excited to have a hedge fund backer, but that said, the banks are being forced out. Someone has to do this," he said.

Ocwen and Nationstar have touted their track records of helping troubled homeowners over the years. Ocwen said it has completed more modifications under the Home Affordable Modification Program, or HAMP, than any other servicer. Executive Chairman William Erbey said last month during a conference call with investors that the company holds more capital against its servicing rights than banks.

Nationstar, majority owned by private-equity firm Fortress Investment Group LLC, has helped 371,000 customers since 2010 through refinancing and other assistance, CEO Jay Bray said in a conference call last month. A spokesman said the company is "highly regulated," noting it is subject to the CFPB, state regulators and other agencies.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
A hedge against irrational humans; COMMENT

By David Oakley, Investment Correspondent 
677 words
10 March 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
06
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

I am writing this column at my dining room table. It is early in the morning and still dark. The birds have started to sing and the cat has been fed. I am ready to write. This is the ideal environment for me to write my column. So it should be good. Or that is the theory.

Like me, more active fund managers are analysing the environment that helps them produce their best work. In their case, their best trades. Last week a new company launched software to help fund managers assess the perfect circumstances for the perfect trade. Man Group , the world's biggest listed hedge fund, and Artemis, the UK-based asset management group, have started using the software, developed by Clare Flynn Levy, a fund manager herself for Morgan Grenfell in the 1990s.

The software enables a fund manager to key in to their computer a variety of information, from the time a trade was made to the mood they were in when they made it. This allows them to track the process surrounding every trade, helping them find a pattern underpinning the best decisions on stock selection.

Simon Savage, a portfolio manager at GLG Partners, a division of Man, who is using the software, says factors such as how many cups of coffee you have drunk and whether you have had a good night's sleep can influence decisions, but these are small effects. The technology should be used mainly to help take instinctive human judgments, which are often biased by recent observation, out of the decision-making process.

A fund manager who has just lost a lot of money is more likely to be risk averse and cautious in making their next trades. In contrast, one who has just made a lot of money is more likely to be gung-ho and take more chances. Mr Savage refers to Nobel Prize winner Daniel Kahneman's 2011 book Thinking, Fast and Slow. The fast system is intuitive and effort free, while the slow system is based on conscious, deductive reasoning and hard work.

Decisions on asset management, Mr Savage says, should be based on the slow system, but the reality is that investment professionals often take decisions that feel instinctively right . The first step, therefore, for a fund manager is to eliminate this fast-thinking mentality to distinguish luck from skill.

A person needs to look objectively at decisions and not attribute all successful trades to skill and all unsuccessful ones to misfortune. Martin Wheatley, chief executive of the Financial Conduct Authority, the UK regulator, says: "One of the most significant challenges . . . is to recognise that we operate within a very human environment; a fallible world."

Using tools such as the software developed by Essentia Analytics, Ms Flynn Levy's company, fund managers can try to take the human out of their behaviour to operate more rationally. In other words, by using systemic analysis, they can hedge against irrational human behaviour.

Of course, human beings cannot entirely wipe out their fallible, human natures, but technology might enable fund managers to stick to patterns that produce their best trades, whether this is how quickly they like to build up a position in a stock or how rigorous they analyse a company's balance sheet.

The success of passive alternatives, which replicate the market, has put active managers under greater pressure to deliver bigger returns. Every active manager, therefore, should consider using technology to improve performance, even if it only gives them a slight extra edge.

As for my performance, the cat has started to distract me by bashing his head against the open lid of my laptop. I have to let him out or I will lose my focus. Fund managers may have to eradicate the human element for the perfect trades. It seems I need to eradicate the cat for the perfect column. My apologies, if I have fallen short.

David Oakley is the FT's investment correspondent

Markets
Mortgage Market Gets Reshuffled; Business of Servicing Home Loans Is Moving Away From Banks

By Stephanie Armour, Andrew R. Johnson And Rob Copeland 
1,207 words
9 March 2014
22:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Washington's effort to push banks out of the mortgage-servicing business is propelling the handling of customers' loans into companies such as hedge funds and nonbank financial firms.

Business
Corporate Economists Are Hot Again; The Shift Comes as Firms Cope With a Glut of Data; Recession 'Laid Bare' Forecasting Mistakes

Bob Tita 
1,250 words
7 March 2014
18:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CLEVELAND Flooded with data, Parker Hannifin Corp. hired a young economist in 2008 to figure out what the information meant to the industrial conglomerate's far-flung operations.

What Ryan Reed told executives in one of his first presentations didn't go over well. Looking at capacity utilization rates the percentage of available production capacity that is actually in use Mr. Reed told executives that sales in the company's automation business would be substantially lower in October. "The guys said: 'That can't be right. October is normally a really good month for us.' "

But Mr. Reed's forecast was indeed correct. October wasn't a good month for automation, or for any of the Cleveland company's business units. The U.S. economy was on the verge of what would become the worst economic convulsion since the Great Depression. "We've lurched from crisis to crisis since then," said Mr. Reed, now 32 years old.

With more data available than ever before and markets increasingly unpredictable, U.S. companies from manufacturers to banks and pharmaceutical companies are expanding their corporate economist staffs. The number of private-sector economists surged 57% to 8,680 in 2012 from 5,510 in 2009, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics. In 2012, Wells Fargo & Co. had one economist in its corporate economics department. Now, it has six.

"A lot of companies have programmers who are able to process big data," said Tom Beers, executive director of the National Association for Business Economics in Washington, a professional organization with about 2,400 members. "But to find a causality between two things and draw a conclusion really takes somebody with an economics background."

Many companies had corporate economists on staff in the volatile 1970s and '80s, but dropped them when the U.S. economy was steady and strong. Information from government agencies, such as industrial output from the Federal Reserve, was plentiful, along with research from private consultants, including Macroeconomic Advisers LLC in St. Louis and IHS Global Insight of Englewood, Colo.

"The reaction in the corporate world was: 'I can get my average GDP forecasts from anybody. Why do I need an economist in my shop?' " said Ellen Hughes-Cromwick, chief economist for Ford Motor Co.

The key to the revival of in-house economists, companies and economists say, is the need to digest huge amounts of data from production volumes in overseas markets to laptop usage in urban areas to determine opportunities and risks for companies' business units, not just in the U.S. but around the world.

Paul Thomas, chief economist for chip maker Intel Corp., said he and his staff look at how and where consumers are using personal computers, laptops and hand-held devices to pinpoint what geographic markets are underserved and which ones are saturated. "It's something we're learning to do that's going to be useful," he said.

The glut of available data is forcing economists to serve as gatekeepers to ensure that disparate units within companies are using the same data sets and information inputs in their forecasting.

Richard DeKaser, a vice president and corporate economist at Wells Fargo, leads a team of eight people, including six economists, who standardize the models and data used to measure risk in different business units, such as mortgage lending and credit cards.

Previously, one unit might base unemployment figures on payroll data, while another would use household surveys. Doing so undermined the accuracy of tests to measure risks for losses and contributed to mistakes in business planning.

"The great recession laid bare a lot of fundamental mistakes that an economist can be useful in preventing," said Mr. DeKaser, who was previously chief economist for National City Bank.

Some corporate economists are also taking higher public profiles to communicate their companies' messages and forecasts. Mark Finley, general manager of global energy markets and U.S. economics for energy company BP PLC, spends about 30% of his time on the road talking with customers, business groups, trade associations and reporters to build consensus for its outlooks on energy demand and pricing. "The economists are supposed to be out there presenting their views," he said.

James Meil, chief economist at diversified manufacturer Eaton Corp., and his staff of four, made 78 formal presentations last year, including one to the Association for Hose and Accessories Distribution. Distributors and suppliers, especially smaller ones, often rely on economists from their bigger customers to help decide whether they should add production capacity.

"There's not readily available market data or trends data available for the hose markets," said Joseph Thompson, executive vice president of the association, which represents manufacturers and distributors of industrial hoses, fittings and fluid-transferring equipment.

Veteran economists like Mr. Meil, who has been with Eaton for 29 years, are consulted on big acquisitions. When Eaton bought Cooper Industries PLC in 2012 for about $11.8 billion, he provided market research for key business segments and forecasts for the combined company's end markets.

Air Products and Chemicals Inc. uses its economist to devise hedges in a more volatile global economic climate. The Pennsylvania-based supplier of industrial gases enters into 10- to 20-year contracts with drug companies, steelmakers and refineries and needs price-escalator clauses to reflect higher production costs, such as energy.

"We set up formulas to where we're just trying to capture the inflationary pressure on the underlying contract," said economist Joseph Cardinale, who recently left Air Products for an economist's job at Illinois-based pharmaceutical maker Abbott Laboratories.

Like all forecasters, corporate economists can miss the mark. In 2012 many company economists expected about 6.5% growth in manufacturing output in Asia. But it ended up being 3.5% due to a sluggish economy in China. "Forecasts by definition are going to be wrong," said Mr. Cardinale.

Retired economist Frank Schott, 87 years old, said new veins of data don't guarantee accuracy in forecasts. "The data are just as recalcitrant as ever to give you answers and the multiplicity of it invites further confusion," he said. "Everybody, including corporate bosses, thinks they're their own best economist."

Before Parker hired Mr. Reed, group presidents provided forecasts. "They weren't very accurate," said Jeff Cullman, president of Parker's hydraulic business. He said Mr. Reed has been able to forecast when hydraulic demand was about to fall, allowing the company to get a head start on scaling back production. "The market turns relatively quickly, but it takes us about three months to get on the brakes,' he said.

Forecasting can be tougher in developing countries where market demand can be difficult to quantify and data is spotty. Mr. Reed recently constructed a model for Parker's new president of Latin America to indicate where the company should focus sales efforts. "His gut was telling him we're doing pretty well in Brazil; maybe we should look more at Argentina," he said.

By parceling out Parker's sales in specific industrial markets in Argentina and Brazil and comparing them with the two countries' total output in those markets, Mr. Reed demonstrated that Parker's sales potential in Brazil was far greater than in Argentina and to remain focused there. "Hopefully we avoided putting a lot of resources into Argentina where there's just not much to capture," he said.

Law Journal: Class Action: Help to Investor?

By Julie Steinberg 
860 words
3 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The Supreme Court this week will consider whether a hotly contested legal theory underpinning most class-action securities suits makes sense.

Meanwhile, the debate over whether such suits benefit investors is equally fraught.

Many investors see class-action suits as important checks on corporate misbehavior. But companies' legal budgets can easily overwhelm individual plaintiffs, and over-stretched regulators can't pursue every allegation of misinformation. When it comes down to dollars and cents, some studies show the suits aren't very good at compensating shareholder losses.

Generally, aggrieved investors get only pennies on the dollar. The average settlement amounts to 1% to 5% of shareholder losses, according to the Stanford Securities Litigation Analytics database, which tracks securities litigation.

The Supreme Court will hear oral argument Wednesday on whether to preserve a legal doctrine known as fraud-on-the-market, which relieves investors from having to prove they relied upon company misinformation. The number of securities class actions has grown considerably in the 25 years the theory has been accepted. The case could give the court a platform for cutting back drastically on such lawsuits, sparking renewed discussions about whether the suits are actually useful.

Critics say that when plaintiffs win or settle cases accusing firms of inflating share prices through false optimism or fraud, those investors are paid off at the expense of others, and lawyers collect much of the bounty.

Typically, the company -- and sometimes its insurance company -- is tasked with paying out the settlement to the aggrieved investors, effectively forcing current shareholders to make other shareholders whole.

Such lawsuits cost investors about $39 billion a year and recover just $5 billion, according to a recent report from Navigant Consulting Inc. on behalf of the U.S. Chamber Institute for Legal Reform, an affiliate of the U.S. Chamber of Commerce that advocates for business interests.

The consulting firm looked at the stock price of various companies during the lawsuit period, lawyers' fees and how the settlement funds were parceled out to investors, among other things.

Some investors aren't happy about the money that lawyers reap from the settlements. "We need to find a way to have the lawyers' fees capped in such a way that the system can't be milked," said Leonard Masiowski, an investor in Hudson, Ohio.

Mr. Masiowski is waiting to receive his part of the more than $2.4 billion Bank of America Corp. agreed to pay out over allegations the Charlotte, N.C., bank misled investors about the health of Merrill Lynch, which it acquired during the financial crisis. Investors "get pennies on the dollar," said Mr. Masiowski, 78 years old. The lawyers "end up with dollars on the dollar."

Between 2011 and 2013, median plaintiff attorneys' fees accounted for 30% of settlements below $5 million, according to a report from NERA Economic Consulting. For settlements over $1 billion, fees were nearly 10%.

In the Bank of America litigation, plaintiff lawyers' fees and expenses came to $177 million as of Dec. 31, 2013, NERA said.

The Securities and Exchange Commission was able to extract only $150 million from the bank in its own lawsuit over similar issues. Max Berger, one of the lawyers who led the $2.4 billion recovery in the shareholder suit, said he sees his lawsuits as a necessary complement to the work the SEC does.

At the time, Bank of America agreed not to deny the SEC's allegations. It acknowledged the agency's statement of facts in the case but said that was not "an admission as to the truth of any such statements," according to a court document. The SEC and a spokesman for the bank declined to comment.

Supporters of the current system say it enables them to challenge companies' misstatements.

The city of Providence, R.I., recently settled with Aropostale Inc. over claims the retailer made positive statements about its performance that masked deeper problems.

"We're happy we recovered funds for the city," said Jeffrey Padwa, the solicitor who brings claims on behalf of the city's pension fund. "Investors aren't going to get a dollar for dollar, but we got something back."

"We have consistently maintained that this lawsuit was meritless and there was no wrongdoing," an Aeropostale spokes- woman said. "A settlement, funded entirely by the company's insurance, ended the distraction of ongoing litigation."

Mr. Padwa said that without class-action certification, it would have been "unaffordable" to bring a claim.

Investors said the lawsuits foster corporate-governance changes at the affected companies and deter others.

Catherine LaMarr, general counsel for the Office of the Connecticut State Treasurer, said the state often participates in such suits. Her office manages the state's pension and trust funds and wants to enforce a level playing field for investors. Between 2001 and June 2013, her office recovered over $43 million in securities lawsuits, she said.

"I like asset recovery wherever we can get it," she added. "If we're going to collect more than the postage, we're going to file the claim form."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Premier worried about Europe; Fund focus

By Philip Scott 
547 words
3 March 2014
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
17
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Region's recovery fails to convince equity manager, writes Philip Scott

Premier Asset Management's global equity manager Jake Robbins has pared back his European exposure and remains unconvinced by the continent's recovery .

The manager of the Premier Global Alpha Growth fund has reduced his European holdings from around 20 per cent to 15 per cent of the portfolio.

"I worry that Europe will disappoint. I feel there is too much weight being placed on its recovery," Mr Robbins says.

While the eurozone economy expanded by 0.3 per cent in the final three months of 2013, up from 0.1 per cent growth the previous quarter, Mr Robbins highlighted December's weak industrial production figures that fell back 0.7 per cent, lower than the consensus estimate of a 0.3 per cent drop.

He inherited the fund in November 2011 when thematic investing guru Mike Jennings, formerly chief investment officer at Premier, quit the company.

At the time the fund was focused on macroeconomic factors in making stockpicking decisions, but Mr Robbins instituted a stronger focus on company fundamental factors. He prefers to use macro factors only to identify areas of potential opportunity.

Under the new combined process, the manager currently considers emerging markets, especially China , to be very attractive in spite of recent turmoil.

He says: "The Chinese market is the cheapest it has ever been and while growth may be slowing, there is still a wealth of great companies housed there."

He highlights China Lesso, the largest water-pipe manufacturer in the country and one of his top-10 plays.

"The theme of growing urbanisation in China means the government needs to bring more water to its cities. If you persevere with that kind of business, you can make money in the long term," the manager says.

Mr Robbins is also backing Truly International, which manufactures and sells liquid crystal display products to China's domestic smartphone market, where it derives 80 per cent of its revenues.

Elsewhere the manager has backed Indian conglomerate Tata, which is the country's largest carmaker. The parent company of Jaguar Land Rover - which it snapped up from Ford in 2008 - is showing " phenomenal growth ", he says.

While he has no holdings in Latin America, almost a third of Mr Robbins' assets are spread across emerging markets and Asia, although his largest weighting is still to the US.

He says: "We will always have a decent weighting to the US as there are a lot of great cash-generative companies out there."

Mr Robbins will mark his third anniversary as manager of the fund this November and has just seen his assets under management surge considerably from 18m to 75m. This follows the fund's merger with the Premier Global Strategic Growth fund, which he used to co-manage with Mr Jennings before his departure.

In the past 12 months to February 14 the Global Alpha Growth fund has returned 5 per cent, underperforming the IMA Global sector average of 8 per cent for the period, according to FE Analytics. In three years it has matched its peer group's 17 per cent gain.

Philip Scott is a freelance journalist for Investment Adviser

FT REPORT - THE CONNECTED BUSINESS
US revelations put heat on business

By Stephen Pritchard 
1,357 words
24 February 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys CBB1
04
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Privacy The NSA leaks and new EU rules will push data protection up the agenda, says Stephen Pritchard

Keeping personal data private is an issue that continues to vex corporations and governments alike.

In the past month alone, retailers Target in the US and UK-based Tesco have suffered data breaches, while Barclays, the UK-based bank, revealed that details of thousands of its investor customers were stolen and sold on .

Meanwhile Edward Snowden's revelations about data collection by the US National Security Agency and other security bodies continue to cast a shadow over the whole question of privacy online.

But then privacy - at least when it comes to the sharing of personal data online - is not a static concept. Attitudes to personal privacy and to data sharing vary widely around the world.

In the US and the UK, citizens are considered to be relatively relaxed about data sharing, although they are perhaps less so post-Snowden.

Germans tend to take a much stricter view on what government and businesses can do with personal data, a fact reflected both in Germany's strict data protection and its employment law.

France, for its part, has a strong legal defence of personal privacy. But there, as elsewhere, the legal framework is coming under pressure from the much more rapid evolution of privacy online.

"Attitudes have changed," says Ronald Koorn, a partner and data privacy expert at KPMG, based in the Netherlands.

"Initially, the NSA incident was primarily a media event, but that discussion is now reaching multinational companies. They realise that privacy requires increased attention.

"It was not previously among their top 10 concerns. In the US, there has been more strict compliance, but in Europe it has been more relaxed - or more lax. That has changed because of the NSA, but also what is happening in Brussels."

As Mr Koorn points out, the EU planned to update its data protection framework well before Mr Snowden's revelations put the matter on the front pages. The existing EU data protection directive - a set of legal principles that are used as the basis of national laws - is due to be replaced by a more binding regulation that will apply across the EU.

The timescale for implementing the new laws has slipped several times; late 2014, or even early 2015, now seems the earliest for a final draft of the new law. When it comes, the regulation will bring far greater consistency across Europe on data privacy and data protection.

But it will impose some strict new standards, in areas such as the disclosure of data breaches, as well as far greater penalties for organisations that transgress. One particularly controversial proposal will give data protection authorities the ability to fine companies up to 5 per cent of their worldwide turnover if they suffer a breach.

Although business leaders might not welcome such moves, experts say it is a necessary response to today's very different climate around personal data.

"We are dealing with a world that has changed so much since the early 1990s, before we even get on to issues such as the cloud or social media," says Vinod Bange, a partner at law firm Taylor Wessing.

"All [those changes] have taken place since the current regime came into force. The current laws, around data processors and data controllers, have not worked. They may have been relevant to the computer bureaux of the 1990s, but no longer."

And, Mr Bange adds, it is right for lawmakers and businesses alike to debate whether the current legal and regulatory framework is effective. "Do we have the balance right? That depends on changing attitudes," he says. "There are rights and obligations around data, but also only to collect data if there are lawful grounds."

But what constitute those lawful grounds is by no means fixed. Individuals have become much more open to sharing some personal information, through social media, but by no means all information.

There are questions, too, of legitimacy and proportion when it comes to collecting data. The Snowden revelations aside, most citizens will accept a greater level of data gathering and even covert data surveillance by law enforcement or security agencies than by local government and health providers. These in turn will have more latitude than private companies.

Some protection specialists see that awareness as a positive development, even if it may bring short-term inconvenience to companies.

"The political climate has resulted in a situation where people have become more aware that data are collected, not just by government but also by business," suggests Harvey Lewis, analytics research director at professional services firm Deloitte. "The concerns are around increasing transparency, and making sure customers are aware of the data that are held on them."

But if opinions about data sharing and data collection are far from static, so is the way those data are being used by governments and companies, and how they should be protected.

The trend for organisations to use more and more sophisticated analysis of huge amounts of data - so called "big data" - is also changing the way they look at information.

From a privacy point of view, being able to combine vast data sets from different sources in close to real time raises some serious issues. Anonymous or innocuous data sets can be combined to become both sensitive and identifiable.

Javvad Malik, a security researcher at analysts The 451 Group, says: "A challenge for legislation is that it is very, very difficult to quantify and contain, or [to judge] when legitimate use of data becomes malicious. It is like chemistry: some elements are stable, but put together they become explosive." Regulators, he says, have to balance ensuring privacy with the need to avoid controls that prevent the legitimate use of data by governments or companies.

Then there is a further challenge: whether the current generation of data protection tools are adequate to protect growing stores of valuable data, against an increasingly hostile cyberspace environment.

"Mobile and the cloud have turned the idea of a perimeter around data on its head," says John Mancini, president and chief executive of AIIM, the information management trade body. "A sound perimeter is no longer enough . . . You have to look at information governance in a holistic way. You cannot just take a 'Maginot Line' approach, as information is scattered across the organisation."

Instead, Mr Mancini says organisations need to be looking at the "big picture risk": reputational and financial damage, as well as the regulatory consequences of a data breach.

This could cause companies to be more cautious about the data they collect, and more open about the consent they seek for collecting it, he says. There could be a shift from legalistic terms and conditions to plain language, but also more consideration about data disposal. Data degrade over time, and the more data an organisation holds, the greater the risk of damage if there is a breach.

Organisations, though, may not be doing enough to protect their data, despite a succession of privacy-related headlines.

"Companies are not doing enough to prevent breaches. Security only gets attention after a breach," says Seth Berman, managing director at risk consultants Stroz Friedberg. "Companies should start by looking at where their data are and how they are protecting them. Do they need so much information, and is there a policy regarding data destruction?"

This point is echoed by John Skipper, a privacy expert at PA Consulting. "We have seen a marked increase in interest in privacy recently, partially triggered by the large amounts of publicity, but also because the EU regulations are on the drawing board," he says. And this will mean a change in management attitudes to data privacy.

"It was rarely a board level responsibility, but more often the responsibility of the chief information officer or the head of IT security," he says. "But it [data privacy] is now recognised as an important part of the relationship with customers. So it is more likely to lie with the chief operating officer, or a business director."

 Big data  bring risks and benefits to insurance customers

By Joe Barnes 
709 words
24 February 2014
06:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Who benefits from big data? As a rising number of companies employ "data mining" to excavate the vast trails of shopping habits and Google searches we leave behind us online, how is that likely to affect the consumer?

The advantages for the company are manifest and widely known: a recent McKinsey Institute report noted that "a retailer using big data to the full could increase its operating margin by more than 60 per cent".

But a rather more controversial example of its proposed use is that of insurance companies desire to create profiles of their prospective policy holders.

This would be particularly welcome in the life insurance sector, where manual methods of underwriting and risk calculation can cost hundreds of pounds to undertake and can drag on for weeks. This is, says a spokesperson for Liverpool Victoria, a British insurer, because they involve "researching medical and family history, whether any of their relatives had suffered with particular illnesses", as well as conducting urine and blood tests.

But could it mean that premiums for individuals may rise on the basis of their browsing history?

On one level, big data could enable insurers to incorporate public information such as social media profiles or work with government data to get to know their customers better, says Mark van Rijmenam, founder of BigData-Startups, an advisory website. "Instead of creating six [generic customer profiles] that cater for millions of customers, they can make one [for each individual] through 360-degree customer profiling," he says.

There can be gains for both parties, adds Richard Sadler, head of retail protection proposition development at Zurich UK Life. Individuals with a healthy lifestyle can be given premium discounts on the basis of data on whether they take part in sports or go to the gym.

This knowledge, adds Colin Forrest, an insurance analyst partner at Deloitte, would enable insurers to eliminate the sometimes "unnecessary and time-consuming" screening processes for low-risk applicants, as well as offering them "significant gains in the pricing of risk".

The tabular content relating to this article is not available to view. Apologies in advance for the inconvenience caused.

<>Mr Forrest believes such practices are just beginning. "The US is further ahead in this respect, because of years more experience in data gathering but there is a growing appetite in the UK and Europe," he says.

In 2013, the average Dutch person's information was available on 1,500 databases, according to Maarten Louman, a partner at the Qiy Foundation, a data storage company in the Netherlands.

The benefits of big data are not limited to those working in the underwriting business. Customers may also benefit from a more streamlined application process and user-friendly websites.

Mr Sadler at Zurich notes that "web analytics software can track how customers navigate through websites, and which pages, or even questions within pages, cause customers to delay or drop out of a process. Insurers are making use of these data to design their websites better, and to make it quicker and simpler for their customers to buy insurance."

Concerns are growing, however, as to whom exactly has access to this information. The increased use of big data has led people to believe - rightly or wrongly - that their privacy is being invaded. Insurers too are aware of the need to balance efficiency gains against the risk of alienating their customers.

"At the moment, the data behind this are still quite basic," says Mr Sadler, "but over time, some insurers may look for permission to get more detailed data on people's habits - for example, by tracking their behaviour from their mobile phones, or using their Google search history to find out their interests - and use this to refine premiums further."

He adds: "This leads to one of the concerns with big data, which is customer data protection. Would consumers be happy for insurers to look through their Facebook photos as part of the underwriting process? Or tracking their every move via a smartphone's location services to confirm whether their behaviour is in line with what they disclosed on the application form?

"As an industry, we need to tread carefully here and demonstrate responsibility."

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - LIFE & ARTS
FT Diary

By Izabella Kaminska 
1,221 words
22 February 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
02
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

I am on the train from Geneva to Zurich, bound for the annual forecasting dinner of the Swiss Chartered Financial Analyst society, where I've been invited to speak about the virtual cryptocurrency Bitcoin. All I know is that the gathering is at the Dolder Grand hotel, that most people will be CFA society members and that I've had about an hour's sleep the night before.

All in all, I have a love-hate relationship with public speaking. On one hand, I love to do it because it's a great honour and a wonderful opportunity to articulate one's views without fear of interruption. On the other hand, I suffer from stage fright.

How monumental the stage fright is depends on a number of variables: how much sleep I've had the night before - itself dependent on how intimidated I am by the event; how familiar I am with the topic I'm discussing; the composition and hostility of the crowd; and, finally, the nature of the appearance.

I make myself do these things, nevertheless, because (a) I'm a glutton for punishment and (b) I've been told that it's only with practice and conditioning that I can suppress my public-speaking demons. And true, sometimes it can turn out absolutely fine. Other times it can be . . . well, awkward.

To the annoyance of most of the train's dining carriage I find myself muttering and gesticulating furiously as I rerun the key points in my head in a vain attempt to memorise some emergency phrases.

The Dolder Grand turns out to be Zurich's most prestigious hotel, which is another way of saying that it's more intimidating than I ever imagined. An impressive "movies are made of this" driveway leads to the gargantuan modernised castle, which sits prominently on a hill overlooking the city. I arrive by taxi. But all around me there are supercars.

The evening air is crisp and my breath lingers as I thank the randomly Russian-speaking taxi driver, and wonder to myself whether I should ask him to pick me up later? After all, how do you get an affordable taxi around here?

As I walk through the corridors on my way to the drinks reception I note that the place is filled to the brim with creepy modern art, illuminated by extremely moody and artistic lighting. Everyone is serious. Everything is sparkly and shiny. Stanley Kubrick's film Eyes Wide Shut rushes to the forefront of my mind.

I'm slightly late, because of a train timetabling error on my part, and the drinks reception is already in full swing by the time I enter what seems to be the gold room. The lack of sleep is beginning to make everything feel very dreamlike and blurry. Like Jack Torrance in The Shining , I would do anything for a drink to relieve my mental block. But I decide it's better to ask for coffee. A bow-tied waiter appears out of nowhere to take my order.

A Swiss-German man tries to explain his Bitcoin valuation model to me. We're being constantly interrupted but I think he's insisting that the currency's value lies in the inflexible supply. I think I hear a reference to Austrian School economics. I can't really make him out because it's noisy and he has an accent. All I know is, if he's any indication of what the audience will be like, I could be entering hostile territory. I seem to fervently disagree with almost all his points.

We proceed to the dining room, where I finally get a good look at the crowd. Men outnumber women at least five to one, if not more. Most are wearing business suits and some even have little CFA society pins on their lapels. Needless to say, I feel like a total outsider.

It's time. I take my position on the podium, gulp down the water provided, and begin.

In hindsight, it is not the best performance of my life. Eventually I see someone making a "cut off'" gesture at me from the audience. The primeval part of my brain interprets this as a final judgement of the society members. I prepare to be ejected from the gathering and never asked back. Thankfully, it turns out that it's just the end of my allocated speaking time and we will now be having questions.

The inquisition phase of the evening goes much better and I realise that I am generally much more confident when responding to direct points. But the next day I'm back in Geneva preparing to do the whole thing again for the CFA society members who live in the French part of Switzerland. The venue this time around is the equally imposing President Wilson hotel on the lake. But this time it's a smaller crowd, and a much more informal setting. I am much more confident. There are also more women in the audience.

A lively question and answer session plus positive feedback after my appearance reassures me that I have probably performed more competently. I put this down to the adage that practice makes perfect, and conclude that I can indeed be cured of my affliction with the correct conditioning.

I have just arrived in Warsaw's Okecie airport on a direct flight from Geneva to visit my Polish family. It's the first day of the Winter Olympics and, as a keen skier, I'm annoyed I've missed the opening ceremony as a result of the flight.

It's pretty late and we are hungry. I'm not optimistic about our chances of finding somewhere to eat that late, but then I remember Warsaw is nothing like Geneva. And true to form, the city is more vibrant and abuzz than it's ever been. We easily find a restaurant open and serving past midnight. And for less than 200 zloty (40) my husband and I eat like kings. To boot there's a rerun of the opening ceremony on the restaurant's TV screens.

My annual Warsaw visit follows a familiar pattern. There are visitations with old friends for reminiscences over coffee and cheese cake. There's a visit to my bridge-playing great aunt, a war hero, who even at the age of 89 is more familiar with current affairs and technology than most thirtysomethings. (This time she informs me I have too many mobile phones and that I should get a handset with two sims so I don't miss her calls. Also that she's suspicious of recent advances in artificial intelligence.)

And finally there's a visit to a now snowy Powazki cemetery, Warsaw's own Pre Lachaise, where hundreds of impressively decorated headstones mark the final resting places of the deceased but are so densely packed you need knowledge of key markers to navigate yourself efficiently or be lost. In my case a giant pyramidal tomb points the way to the granite obelisk that marks my mother's grave and commemorates the date 2011. I think to myself that she would have liked my story about the Dolder Grand.

Izabella Kaminska is a reporter for FT Alphaville

Moody's Offers to Increase Its Stake in India's ICRA to 55%; ICRA is a Provider of Credit Ratings and Research in Region's Emergings Markets

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Moody's Corp. unveiled plans to seek a majority stake in ICRA Ltd., a provider of credit ratings and research in India, through a tender offer.

Moody's, a ratings and analytics company, offered to acquire up to 2.65 million shares of ICRA at a price of 2,000 Indian rupees (about $32.19) a share, a 26% premium over ICRA's closing price on the National Stock Exchange of India Ltd. on Thursday. The offer represents a 22% premium to ICRA's record-high closing price at the end of last year, Moody's said.

Full acceptance of the offer would boost Moody's ownership stake in ICRA to about 55%, from 28.5% currently, Moody's said in a statement. The offer is conditional upon Moody's acquiring at least 2.15 million shares, which would increase its ownership stake to slightly more than 50%.

Moody's, which relies on its debt-rating operation for the vast majority of its income, said it would fund the offer from international cash on hand and the tender period is expected to begin in April, subject to review of the deal by Indian regulatory authorities.

"We look forward to expanding and deepening our collaboration with ICRA as it provides research and ratings for the growing domestic debt market in India as well as other emerging markets in the region," said Moody's President and Chief Executive Raymond McDaniel.

Earlier this month, Moody's reported its fourth-quarter profit jumped as revenue grew in its structured finance segment. The company's shares have risen about 6% in the past three months.

Former Citigroup CEO Plans New Consulting Firm

By Jenny Strasburg and Ianthe Jeanne Dugan 
625 words
21 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
18
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Less than two years after he was ousted as chief executive of Citigroup Inc., Vikram Pandit is collaborating with the co-author of the popular "Freakonomics" book series to launch a new consulting business aimed at helping companies improve their cultures and use of technology.

Mr. Pandit and his new colleagues have been ringing up bank chief executives and others and offering their services. Among the recent recipients of the pitch: Top executives at Deutsche Bank AG, which is trying to improve its corporate culture amid a number of regulatory and legal problems.

The firm, called TGG, is promoting "a novel approach to address the challenges that large complex organizations face in compliance, fraud, corruption, and culture and reputation," said an email to Deutsche executives from Hamid Biglari, one of Mr. Pandit's former Citigroup lieutenants and a managing partner of the new venture. "We think the results are transformative for companies looking to create a culture of performance and integrity," the email said.

Another plus, according to the email: Messrs. Pandit and Biglari's "domain expertise" from their time running Citigroup.

The email, reviewed by The Wall Street Journal, proposes that Messrs. Pandit and Biglari, whose venture is based in Midtown Manhattan, fly to Frankfurt or London for lunch with the Deutsche Bank executives. It isn't clear whether the rendezvous will actually happen. A Deutsche Bank spokesman declined to comment on internal communications. Mr. Pandit wasn't available to comment.

Mr. Biglari's email says TGG's partners include Steven Levitt, co-author of the "Freakonomics" series that combines economics with everyday decision-making, and Daniel Kahneman, a winner of the Nobel Prize in economics.

Messrs. Levitt and Kahneman already helped create a Chicago-based consulting firm called The Greatest Good. A planned partnership between TGG and The Greatest Good isn't yet completed, according to people with knowledge of the entities' structure. Messrs. Levitt and Kahneman didn't respond to emails requesting comment.

The venture is the latest effort by the 57-year-old Mr. Pandit to bounce back after leaving Citigroup. The New York banking giant's board removed him in 2012 due to frustration with, among other things, his management style and his frosty relationship with regulators, people familiar with private discussions said at the time.

These days, Mr. Pandit is experimenting with roles ranging from venture capitalist to deal adviser to consultant, say people familiar with his activities. One of his ventures is funding a peer-to-peer lending business called the Orchard.

Last year, Mr. Pandit called bank senior executives, including Credit Suisse Group AG CEO Brady Dougan, as part of an effort to create a bank consortium to buy stock-exchange data and technology assets, according to people close to the communications.

In that effort, Mr. Pandit worked with a former Morgan Stanley colleague, banker Charles Stonehill. Discussions about a potential consortium didn't progress toward a deal, the people said. Mr. Stonehill is now working with Messrs. Pandit and Biglari on TGG.

TGG's aim is to use insights into human behavior, economics and so-called big data -- the crunching of ever-greater volumes of information more quickly and deeply -- to help large, complex companies analyze employee behavior, management decision-making, business models and strategy. Those are areas where Messrs. Levitt and Kahneman should add value.

TGG doesn't have any clients yet, but Messrs. Pandit and Biglari plan to use their networks of CEO contacts around the world to identify prospective customers in the banking industry and beyond, according to a person with knowledge of their plans.

Risk management: is your supply chain a weak link?

By Steve Durbin, 
1,107 words
20 February 2014
22:35
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Supply chains are the backbone of today's global economy. Their complexity and vital role have businesses increasingly concerned about managing major disruptions.

In fact, a recent World Economic Forum report (in co-operation with Accenture) entitled "Building Resilience in Supply Chains", indicates that significant supply chain disruptions reduce the share price of affected companies by as much as seven per cent on average.

Security chiefs everywhere are concerned about how open their supply chain is to various risk factors. Indeed, it is important to remember that almost all aspects of business involve some risk, in the sense that some components will always be outside of your control, especially in a global marketplace.

Your supply chain is no different; it is one of the most collaborative environments in your organisation, thus it inherently poses greater risks to the confidentiality, integrity and availability of corporate information. Mapping the flow of information and keeping an eye on key access points in order to continuously manage information security risks is an essential part of building a more resilient business.

Hands-on risk management: do you know where your information is?

Do you know if your suppliers are protecting your company's sensitive information as diligently as you would protect it yourself? This is one duty you can't simply outsource - it's your liability. By considering the nature of your supply chains, determining what information is shared, and assessing the probability and impact of potential breaches, you can balance information risk management efforts across your enterprise.

Organisations of all sizes need to think about the consequences of a supplier providing accidental, but harmful, access to their corporate information. Information shared in the supply chain can include intellectual property, customer or employee data, commercial plans or negotiations, and logistics. Caution should not be confined to manufacturing or distribution partners. It should also embrace your professional services suppliers (eg, lawyers and accountants) all of whom share access, often to your most valuable data assets.

To address information risk in the supply chain, organisations should adopt robust, scalable and repeatable processes - obtaining assurance proportionate to the risk faced. Supply chain information risk management should be embedded within existing procurement and vendor management processes, so supply chain information risk management becomes part of regular business operations.

Secure your supply chain: three vulnerabilities to examine

There are three key reasons organisations may find they are vulnerable due to information sharing across their supply chain:

1. Lack of awareness of the sensitive information being shared in contracts

2. Too many contracts to assess individually

3. Lack of visibility and controls as information is shared in the supply chain

Some organisations focus on the first reason, and assess the information risk for each contract. This approach does not address the second issue, as it is not scalable for organisations with thousands of contracts. The third vulnerability, the most challenging and urgent for many businesses, is even more complex to address. Organizations typically have no relationship with their suppliers' suppliers, so the risk increases as visibility and influence decrease upstream.

Collaborate securely to empower and protect your business

Accelerating trends of supply chain globalisation and outsourced manufacturing and distribution have combined to increase the pace of change, complexity, and risk for brand owners. These trends have created a fundamental shift in the way companies of all sizes plan, source, make, and deliver their goods and services.

Enabling integration with supply chain partners, and then facilitating real-time visibility and collaboration capabilities, should your first objectives. The ability to see, collaborate and resolve disruptions as they occur in your network is what defines a risk-resistant supply chain. When one part of the supply chain is disrupted, the effects can lead to issues throughout your entire supply chain network.

Technologies and systems solutions, such as those from E2open and Kinaxis, use real-time information, integrated business processes and advanced analytics to provide businesses with visibility into, and control over, their supply chains.

Supply Chain Information Risk Assurance Process (SCIRAP)

The Information Security Forum (ISF) has created the Supply Chain Information Risk Assurance Process (Scirap), an approach that helps organisations manage information risk across their supplier base. Scirap focuses on identifying information shared in the supply chain and the contracts that create the highest risk. It also provides a scalable way to manage contracts so that efforts are proportionate to the risk.

Many organisations have contracts with suppliers that number in the hundreds or thousands. It is often impossible to commit enough resources to assess all those contracts and the associated suppliers. Scirap provides a practical method to sort existing contracts into groups in order to prioritise efforts based on information risk.

Scirap focuses on information shared with upstream suppliers. The approach suggests that organisations make use of supply chain maps to follow the information. An upstream information sharing assessment tracks what is being shared with the suppliers' suppliers and beyond. The results will draw attention to significant concentrations of information, triggering the implementation of additional controls on suppliers.

Scirap integrates with existing procurement and vendor management processes, providing a mechanism to make risk management a part of normal business operations. As a result, organisations of all sizes will be able to more effectively identify and prioritise the assurance or actions required.

Be Prepared, Stay Resilient

Even the smallest supplier, or the slightest supply chain hiccup, can have dangerous impacts on your business. Brand management and brand reputation are subject to the successful security of your supply chain and thus both are constantly at stake.

The time to make supply chain security enhancements a priority is now. As the old saying goes, an ounce of prevention is worth a pound of cure. ISF's Scirap approach provides a great example of a detailed, four step approach to portion an otherwise daunting project into manageable components. This method is information-driven (not supplier-centric) so it is scalable and repeatable across the enterprise.

Don't wait for demand to spike, or the next disaster to strike, before taking action. Businesses of all sizes must focus fixes on the most vulnerable spots in their supply chain now. The unfortunate reality of today's complex global marketplace is that not every security compromise can be prevented beforehand; being proactive now also means you - and your suppliers - will be better able to react quickly and intelligently when something does happen. In extreme but entirely possible scenarios, this readiness and resiliency may dictate competitiveness, financial health, share price, or even survival.

Steve Durbin is global vice-president of the Information Security Forum (ISF).

pict038.png

Global Finance: Fed, European Banks Face Off on New Rules

By Geoffrey Smith, Ryan Tracy and Stephanie Armour 
816 words
20 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

European banks and the Federal Reserve began a chess match over new rules for U.S. units of foreign banks, with overseas firms expected to try and limit the impact and Fed officials vowing to prevent banks from evasion.

The jockeying over the Fed's new rules could further ruffle tensions between the U.S. and Europe over what European officials see as a unilateral abandonment of a globally agreed-to approach for financial regulation.

The Fed's move comes days before finance ministers and central-bank governors from the Group of 20 leading industrialized and emerging markets are due to meet in Sydney, Australia.

The G-20 has found it increasingly hard to keep a common line on regulation as the effect of the 2008 financial crisis has faded.

The Fed Tuesday formally approved new rules requiring the largest international banks operating in the U.S. to set up U.S.-based "intermediate holding companies," which will be subject to the same capital and liquidity requirements as domestic banks.

The move ends an arrangement whereby the U.S. and European Union effectively trusted each other to keep their respective banks sound.

"This is a considerable competitive handicap for European banks, as their U.S. competitors aren't subject to any equivalent requirements in the EU," said Michael Kemmer, head of the Association of German Banks.

Sebastien de Brouwer, head of legal affairs at the European Banking Federation, said the move risks creating a standards gap between the U.S. and Europe, one that would duplicate reporting requirements, fragment liquidity in international markets and again incentivize banks to engage in "regulatory arbitrage" by moving their businesses to locations with the least regulation.

Fed governor Daniel Tarullo argued Tuesday that "the most important contribution we can make to the global financial system is to ensure the stability of the U.S. financial system."

The Fed has vowed to keep a close eye on European banks, which -- troubled by the Fed's growing reach into international banking regulation -- may take steps to curb the Fed's reach by moving some activity into other parts of their operations or otherwise circumscribe the mandate for adding certain types of capital. Buried in the documents released Tuesday are provisions saying the Fed could accelerate compliance deadlines for banks that try to circumvent the rules or impose additional "restrictions the Board deems necessary to carry out the purposes" of the rule, such as even stricter capital and leverage limits.

"We will be monitoring pretty carefully how foreign banks adapt to our proposal," Mark Van Der Weide, deputy director of the Fed's Division of Banking Supervision and Regulation, said at the Fed meeting Tuesday.

Many analysts were more sanguine on the rules' impact. Kian Abouhossein, an analyst with J.P. Morgan in London, said many details of the final Fed rule were less stringent than what had been originally proposed.

He noted that banks could use funding from their global holding companies to bolster capital at their U.S. units.

Deutsche Bank AG, one of the biggest foreign banks in the U.S., could comfortably meet the Fed's requirements without having to take any further measures on capital and leverage beyond what it already has announced, he said.

The Fed, in the rule released Tuesday, said some foreign banks will be able to meet the new capital requirements for their U.S. subsidiaries by retaining more earnings in the U.S. or transferring capital from their parents, rather than raising capital externally or reducing their U.S. activity.

But the Fed also sought to limit banks' flexibility. "They're really trying to send a message [that] 'if you take us on, we will shut you down,'" said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy-analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

While the Fed didn't spell out whether specific securities would qualify as capital under the rule, the regulator defined capital for foreign banks in the same way it does for U.S. firms. To be considered Tier 1, a capital instrument would have to meet a series of criteria. For instance, the underlying contract can't require the bank to reimburse the investor on a set date, and it must be subordinate to other creditors if the bank hits rocky times. So long as foreign banks add capital instruments that meet the Fed's definition, they would comply with the rule.

Fed officials have said they would monitor foreign banks and won't shy away from stepping in if changes need to be made to ensure foreign banks comply. That may further ruffle the European Union, which has bristled at U.S. requirements they see as putting foreign banks at a competitive disadvantage.
