Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- The Experts: Insights From Our Online Panel

1,834 words
4 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The start of a new year is always a good time to re-evaluate one's financial strategies, and the new tax season brings tax concerns to the fore. We asked The Experts for investment ideas that tend to get overlooked and for tax tips for fund investors. The Experts are an exclusive online group of industry and thought leaders who comment on topics raised in the Investing in Funds & ETFs and Wealth Management reports. We also asked them to share some of the best -- and worst -- New Year's financial resolutions they've made. Edited excerpts follow. For more, visit WSJ.com/WealthReport.

What little-known investment should people turn to more often?

Best Long-Term Investment: Your Job

Your most important financial asset is your job, and even a small increase in your earning ability can make a huge difference. Imagine you are 30 and making $70,000 a year. If you get a 3% increase each year, by the time you're 65, you'll have made $4.2 million. Alternatively, what if you invest in some classes? What if you get your C.F.A., M.B.A. or some other degree? What if you can up your increases to 5% a year? Over that same period, you'll earn $6.3 million.

-- Matt Hougan, president of analytics and publications, ETF.com

Benefits of Commercial Real Estate

Consider real-estate investment trusts or possibly some commingled funds that invest in commercial real estate. This asset class is relatively easy to understand, tends to pay a high dividend and can generate additional return through capital appreciation. These types of investments might be especially appropriate for investors near and in retirement.

-- Larry D. Zimpleman, chairman, president and chief executive, Principal Financial Group

Small-Cap, Value-Oriented Stocks

Individuals' biggest advantage in the stock market comes from small, value-oriented stocks. Companies with market capitalizations of $1 billion or less.

First, small-cap value stocks have realized annualized long-term returns of 13.9%, according to the Ibbotson SBBI Classic Yearbook. This is more than 26% better than the long-term returns for large-cap value and more than 50% better than the long-term returns for small-cap growth and large-cap growth stocks.

Second, many institutional investors either cannot invest in companies below a certain size or are limited in how much they can allocate to small-cap stocks. These restrictions cause small-cap stocks to be mispriced more often since there are fewer people analyzing them. Mispricing can lead a stock to being undervalued.

Small-cap stocks aren't without their risks. However, there is still enough upside that it makes sense to consider small-cap, value-oriented stocks.

-- Charles Rotblut, vice president, American Association of Individual Investors

Muni-Bond Mutual Funds

Low-fee municipal-bond mutual funds. Traditionally municipal bonds have been viewed as an investment for people with high incomes. Today, the yield differential between tax-free interest income and taxable interest income benefits people in lower-income tax brackets as well.

-- Rick Ferri, founder, Portfolio Solutions LLC, author and blogger

Two Options for Emergency Savings

You can find federally insured accounts, often with very minimal opening deposits, on Bankrate.com. Another option is what is known as a high-yield, or rewards, checking account. These also carry federal deposit insurance and are completely liquid, but pay higher yields to account holders that are diligent about meeting the monthly requirements -- typically direct deposit, online statements and 10 debit-card transactions a month. The higher payout is typically capped at a balance of $20,000 or $25,000, but for savvy savers who are diligent about having a direct deposit that offsets the amount withdrawn through debit-card transactions, this option can provide a nice lift to the yield you'd otherwise earn on your cash.

-- Greg McBride, senior vice president, chief financial analyst, Bankrate.com

What is your most important tax advice for mutual-fund investors?

How Fund Investors Blow It With Taxes

You still own mutual funds? Really? Are you still watching "Ghostbusters" on your Betamax? You shouldn't own mutual funds in taxable accounts. They are fine products for tax-deferred accounts. They play a key role in my retirement portfolio. But in taxable accounts, actively managed mutual funds are a tax disaster because each year they pay out billions of dollars in capital-gains distributions to their shareholders . . . gains on which you have to pay taxes.

-- Matt Hougan

Look at a Fund's Turnover Ratio

Pay attention to turnover ratio. This percentage figure tells you how much a portfolio has changed over the course of a year. While capital losses offset gains, net capital gains are distributed to investors, which is a taxable event.

-- Charles Rotblut

Don't Let Taxes Rule Investments

Avoid taking short-term capital gains on the sale of appreciated shares. If you're going to sell and don't have realized losses to offset a gain, wait for at least one year and one day to qualify for long-term capital-gain status on the sale. This will save you money on income taxes.

-- Rick Ferri

Focus on What Fund Goes Where

Where you park certain investments can enhance your tax efficiency and net returns, by putting higher-taxed collectibles such as precious-metals funds and income-producing bond funds in a tax-advantaged account, and holding those investments prone to long-term capital gains in a taxable account. Using any capital losses to offset short-term capital gains and up to $3,000 of income can also be a prudent strategy.

-- Greg McBride

Two Simple Ways to Keep More Gains

Taxes appear likely to rise in coming years. Tax-efficient mutual funds or the tax-advantaged structure of an exchange-traded fund are two simple ways to keep more investment gains.

-- Christian Magoon, founder, chief executive, YieldShares, an income-focused ETF sponsor

Look at Embedded Gains and Losses

The most important tax issue to be aware of when investing in mutual funds is the potential of an embedded gain. With the market heading into the fifth year of a bull market, many funds find themselves with potentially significant embedded gains; a big distribution can push an investor into a higher capital-gains tax bracket.

It is also worth noting that the pendulum can swing both ways. After a bear market, mutual funds may have embedded losses, reducing the exposure to capital-gains distributions for the foreseeable future and effectively improving the tax efficiency of holding the fund (though ultimately, any gains must still be reckoned with when ultimately sold).

Evaluating the prospective embedded gains (or losses) within a mutual fund is crucial.

-- Michael Kitces, partner and director of research, Pinnacle Advisory Group

Before Buying a Fund, Check This Date

If you are getting ready to make a mutual-fund purchase, check the mutual-fund company's website to learn its year-end distribution dates. You should delay the purchase after this date (called the ex-dividend date) to avoid paying more taxes on an investment you just made.

-- George Papadopoulos, fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich.

Your most and least successful New Year's financial resolutions?

Emotions Can Derail Resolutions

New Year's resolutions have often helped me get back to the basics of disciplined investing. One year, I resolved to review my investments once a quarter in order to be less concerned about market fluctuations. The resolution helped me refocus on my long-term investment plan.

One resolution that didn't turn out so well was to rebalance my portfolio after an especially strong year. To my surprise, I found myself emotionally attached to several top performers. That emotion led me to only halfheartedly rebalance my portfolio. The following year, several of the winners performed like losers and vice versa.

-- Christian Magoon

Ways to Change Investment Behavior

Every year, I try to improve the organizational part of my finances. I give thought to what mistakes I've made, where I've spent too much time pondering a decision, what I'm overlooking and what my behavioral tendencies are. Then I think about things I can do to alter my approach.

Some things are simple. I have reminders in my calendar to check my credit reports periodically. Others are systems and steps that evolve over time, such as the notes I keep regarding how I manage my portfolio. I keep a log of investing decisions. I maintain a list of my overall portfolio rules. I periodically update this list to account for anything I find that I haven't adequately clarified to myself.

If safeguards and systems are set up to help steer a person, change becomes easier. Start with one behavior (e.g., an annual reminder in your calendar to increase your retirement contributions 0.5 percentage point) and go from there. You'll be amazed at the long-term benefits.

-- Charles Rotblut

The Brutally Hard Resolution

By far and away the New Year's financial resolution that has had the biggest impact on my personal finances is to rebalance my portfolio to bring it back in line with my target asset allocation. Taking this step is psychologically brutal in that it requires you to sell by definition what has been "doing well" and to buy what has been lagging. Additionally, if you are doing this in your taxable accounts there are actual cash consequences when you realize gains that can create another psychological hurdle to get over.

That said, if you have done a solid job of assessing your current and future spending needs and have crafted an investment policy statement to support those needs -- you owe it to yourself to stick to your plan.

-- Manisha Thakor, founder and chief executive, MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC

'Don't Give Up'

In 2009, I resolved to write a book for women on personal finance, and handed it in to my editor that August. In 2010, I committed to getting a second property I owned ready for rental, and was able to take a nice depreciation deduction against rental income on my tax return the following April. At the top of my 2011 list was my intention to run a marathon. That October I finished the Marine Corps Marathon and raised $20,000 for the National Hospice Foundation.

All of these "successes" were also complete failures as resolutions go. Each of them had been on my New Year's list not just once, but again and again, as I had to carry them forward, unaccomplished in the previous year, to the next.

While I will still continue to put big, audacious goals on my annual resolution list, I have also learned to add these more humble additions: "Don't give up" and "Be patient."

-- Eleanor Blayney, consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards

Corporate News -- Microsoft CEO Choice: Opting for Continuity

By Shira Ovide and Spencer E. Ante 
969 words
4 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
21
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Microsoft Corp.'s directors could have anointed a visionary outsider to shake up a technology giant that has fallen behind. Instead, they have opted for a safe choice.

Directors are negotiating with 22-year company veteran Satya Nadella to succeed Chief Executive Steve Ballmer, according to a person familiar with the matter. If the board finalizes the pick in coming days, Mr. Nadella will become the third person to lead Microsoft since Bill Gates and Paul Allen founded the company in 1975.

Mr. Nadella has signaled a desire for continuity, telling directors that, as CEO, he hopes to lean on Mr. Gates, according to several people familiar with the matter. Little in Mr. Nadella's public history at Microsoft suggests he will break from the company's pattern as a fast follower, rather than a trend setter.

"It is clearly a safe pick," says Dan Fletcher, a research analyst at Neuberger Berman LLC's Large Cap Disciplined Growth, a Microsoft investor. Mr. Nadella's selection will disappoint those seeking "an outsider who would drive significant and bold change," he said.

That assessment could be short-lived, however. The job of CEO can embolden executives in unexpected ways. People who have worked with Mr. Nadella credit him with repairing technical glitches at Microsoft's Web-search service, disarming Silicon Valley technologists suspicious of the company and fostering collaboration in a turf-conscious workplace.

If he is named CEO, Mr. Nadella would inherit many advantages. Microsoft generated $27 billion in operating profit in the year ended June 30, and the company holds $84 billion in cash. Microsoft's Windows still runs roughly nine out of every 10 desktop and laptop computers in the world, and its Office and Exchange programs are corporate mainstays.

Most of Mr. Nadella's experience is in serving corporate customers -- the source of two-thirds of Microsoft profits -- which suggests the company may want to double down in that area. He has been a leading internal champion of Microsoft's efforts to rent computing power to other companies, even though that business competes with some of Microsoft's most-profitable products.

Some investors, including an activist firm that soon will have a Microsoft board seat, want Microsoft to focus on corporate software and think the company should shed consumer-oriented operations such as the Xbox videogame business and Bing search engine.

Messrs. Gates and Ballmer and some directors have resisted that course, arguing that lines are blurring between corporate and consumer technology.

Not long ago, Microsoft could afford to fall behind, then spend whatever was necessary to catch up. That's how it unseated Netscape Communications Corp. as the leading Internet browser in the 1990s.

Now, the game has changed. Microsoft is fighting on multiple fronts against well-heeled rivals. It must simultaneously compete with Apple Inc. in smartphones, Google Inc. in Web search, Amazon.com Inc.'s in Web-computing services, Oracle Corp. in databases, Salesforce.com Inc. in Internet-based corporate software and Sony Corp. in videogames, among others.

Mobile phones have been a particular problem. Microsoft's software is on fewer than 4% of smartphones world-wide. Its latest effort to extend its influence is a pending $7.3 billion deal to buy Nokia Corp.'s mobile-phone business, a bet on combining Microsoft's software with Nokia's hardware. That deal, struck just as Mr. Ballmer was cementing plans to step down, saddles Mr. Nadella with a new strategy and 32,000 new employees.

A look at Mr. Nadella's decisions in his current job -- leading the Microsoft division that makes technology to run corporate computer servers and other back-end technology -- reveals an executive who has been willing to buck Microsoft's traditions, to a point.

Mr. Nadella allowed outside developers to use non-Microsoft programming languages, popular with younger coders, on Microsoft's "cloud" service that rents computing power to others. Mr. Nadella's choice was "heretical," Bill Hilf, a former Microsoft manager who then worked in the cloud unit, said in late 2012.

Forrester Research analyst Ted Schadler credits Mr. Nadella with boosting sales and securing capital to build computer server farms that support the company's cloud business. "He's clearly a change agent," Mr. Schadler says.

The impact is measurable, if small by Microsoft standards: In the six months ended Dec. 31, Microsoft's revenue from computer technology and services sold over the Internet more than doubled to $1.1 billion.

In general, though, people who know or have worked with Mr. Nadella say he has followed Microsoft's conservative catch-up playbook, rather than make breakaway bets on new technology.

In 2009, he led Microsoft's effort to revamp Bing to better compete with Google. The new version was technologically adroit, but little different than Google, which remains the dominant search engine. Microsoft executives shy away from discussing market share in search, preferring to refer to Bing as the company's "Big Data engine."

At the cloud unit, Mr. Nadella shifted direction of a product that he called too bold, to make it conform more closely to Amazon's market-leading Web-services offerings. That boosted demand for the floundering Microsoft Web service, but also left an opening for rivals like Salesforce to make their own innovations.

A Microsoft spokesman said the company added additional capabilities to meet market demand.

Jeremy Howard, an entrepreneur now running computer-data firm Strategic Data Science, said Mr. Nadella's shift was sensible, but betrayed a lack of confidence in Microsoft's engineering prowess.

"The old Microsoft would have been, 'We have the best platform here, we'll keep trying,'" he said.

David Benoit contributed to this article.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Jupiter's Financial Opportunities fund rallies; Fund focus

By Nick Reeve 
519 words
3 February 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
21
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Manager 'excited' after refocusing on fundamentals, writes Nick Reeve

Guy de Blonay, the financials stockpicker, says he is excited about the prospects for the once-great Jupiter Financial Opportunities fund after years of lacklustre returns.

The manager says he has "rarely been so pleased" with the 536m portfolio since he became a named manager in 2010, hailing a decision to refocus the fund on his bread-and-butter interest in company fundamentals , rather than macroeconomic factors.

He says he is "excited about the prospects unfolding" for the portfolio, which returned 792 per cent in the 13 years to June 2010 under former manager Philip Gibbs.

The fund was one of the driving forces behind Jupiter's long-term transition from a small, London-based "fund boutique" focusing on wealthy private clients to a European asset management brand.

Swiss-born Mr de Blonay was a protg of macroeconomic guru Mr Gibbs in the early years of his career, before joining the now-defunct New Star Asset Management in 2001 to take on his own financials funds.

After New Star was sold in 2009, Mr de Blonay returned to Jupiter, becoming a named co-manager with Mr Gibbs on Financial Opportunities in June 2010.

But Mr Gibbs's focus on investing based on macro factors dominated the fund and after the credit crunch he remained defensively positioned, missing out on much of the post-2009 market rallies.

During the period the two managers ran the fund, from June 2010 to when Mr Gibbs stepped down in October 2011, it lost 19.1 per cent versus an MSCI AC Financials index loss of just 7.6 per cent in sterling terms, according to FE Analytics, the data provider.

After Mr Gibbs left and Mr de Blonay was joined by current co-manager Robert Mumby, the team refocused on company fundamentals.

"In the early stages after I took over, the fund was influenced by a macro decision that was not favourable - we underestimated the power of central banks to influence markets," says Mr de Blonay.

"In the past two years, we decided to re-emphasise our bottom-up analysis, with less importance placed on the macro. This stockpicking approach has helped in the past 12-18 months."

In the past year, the fund has gained 15.5 per cent compared with an index gain of 8.5 per cent, boosted by a strong past two months, the data show.

"I have rarely been so pleased with the types of companies and themes in the portfolio as in the past year or so," Mr de Blonay says.

The manager does not rule out a return to the fund's previous macro focus, but he says the investment environment is unlikely to return to the difficulties it faced in 2008-09.

He says: "There is always a possibility that we come back to a difficult environment, but I would be very surprised indeed if we have another crisis of this magnitude."

Nick Reeve is deputy news editor at Investment Adviser

COMPANIES
Zynga pounces to snatch UK's Clumsy Ninja app developer; TECHNOLOGY

By Tim Bradshaw in San Francisco and Sally Davies in London 
725 words
31 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed3
21
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Zynga is to pay more than $500m to buy NaturalMotion, the UK developer of mobile apps such as Clumsy Ninja and CSR Racing , in the social gaming company's largest acquisition.

The news came at the same time as Zynga announced a fresh round of job cuts and forecast-busting earnings. Zynga swung back to a $0.03 per share loss in its fourth quarter, after adjusting for certain charges such as stock-based expenses and asset writedowns, it said yesterday, compared with $0.01 earnings a year ago. However, the losses were narrower than had been expected and its shares leapt by 22 per cent in after-hours trading.

NaturalMotion, backed by Balderton Capital, is the second British technology company to be acquired by a larger US firm this week, following Google's $660m purchase of DeepMind , an artificial intelligence company that had also been developing games.

Like DeepMind's founders, Torsten Reil, NaturalMotion's co-founder and chief executive, has a background outside the tech industry, having studied biology at Oxford university before going on to research a PhD in complex systems in the university's zoology department.

Zynga will pay $391m in cash and 38.9m Zynga shares to buy NaturalMotion, with offices in Oxford, London, Brighton and San Francisco, to expand its portfolio of games into racing and people simulation.

"Our acquisition of NaturalMotion will allow us to significantly expand our creative pipeline, accelerate our mobile growth and bring next-generation technology and tools to Zynga that we believe will fast-track our ability to deliver more hit games," said Don Mattrick, Zynga chief executive.

Zynga has struggled to translate its once-popular Facebook-based games such as FarmVille to smartphones and tablets, prompting a sequence of acquisitions of mobile app developers including Newtoy, maker of Words With Friends , and OMGPop, creator of Draw Something .

NaturalMotion has found greater success on mobile. CSR Racing , a driving game for the iPhone and iPad, was at one point in 2012 the highest-grossing app on Apple's App Store. Clumsy Ninja , a character with lifelike animation which players must train like a virtual pet, was released last year.

NaturalMotion grew out of its founders' efforts to create software that mimicked animal movements. Like DeepMind, it relies on computer systems modelled on living creatures, which it then uses to make animated characters perform tasks.

"Basically, we start out with a rag doll," Mr Reil said when NaturalMotion first launched as a spinout from Oxford university. "We then add muscles and a brain to the thing."

The company's technology is used by the developers of the popular but violent video game Grand Theft Auto, to create realistic effects.

Mr Reil's 8 per cent stake in NaturalMotion has netted him $42m as part of the acquisition, according to a source close to the deal. Balderton has a 24 per cent stake, with US venture capital firm Benchmark owning 20 per cent and Oxford university under 11 per cent.

The buyout underscores a growing interest in "deep tech" coming out of UK universities. The sale has been in motion since at least December.

The acquisition comes as Zynga continues to lose players from its existing games at a rapid clip. The number of people paying for virtual items and other in-game items fell by more than half to 1.3m in the fourth quarter of 2013, from 2.9m a year earlier.

As a result, revenues fell by 43 per cent to $176m for the fourth quarter, even as net losses narrowed to $25m from $49m a year ago.

As part of a $35m cost reduction plan this year, Zynga said that it would lay off about 314 employees and cut its datacentre investments. It laid off 18 per cent of its workforce, or 520 people, last June.

 "Aim-quoted Top Level Domain Holdings which is competing against Google and Amazon for control of new web suffixes such as ".app" and ".cloud" has raised $33m from investors. The Dublin company will announce today that it has completed a capital raising that brings its cash balance to $48m in the run-up to auctions of some of the most coveted new domains.

Twitter's Super Bowl Goal: More Ad Credibility

By Yoree Koh and Suzanne Vranica 
938 words
31 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

On Super Bowl Sunday, Twitter will again serve as a grand stage for advertisers to promote brands to millions of people captivated by gameplay, half-time shows and slick commercials.

But Sunday's game also will be a litmus test for whether Twitter Inc. can persuade marketers to spend more money to have a presence on the service instead of simply sending out free tweets, hoping their hashtag-stamped witticisms go viral.

In preparation, Twitter has been offering consulting services, grounded in analytics, which it says can lead to smarter tweeting.

On game day, Twitter will feed data to brands like Unilever PLC's Axe deodorant to gauge reaction to ads and to predict the next Internet meme before it happens. It will also suggest targeted keywords for ads based on conversation topics, such as blasting tweets to Bruno Mars's fans when he sings at halftime.

For the past few months, Twitter's staffers have huddled with Unilever and other large advertisers including PepsiCo Inc. and Bank of America Corp. to share more detailed analysis of user data, and to help with Super Bowl ad campaigns earlier than in previous years.

Twitter isn't charging the marketers for its consulting. The hope is these advertisers will feel more comfortable with Twitter as an ad platform and eventually steer more of their marketing budgets that way.

Twitter, which touts itself as the world's "town square," faces pressure from investors to show it can sell more ads and do it for the long haul. Twitter issues its first quarterly report as a public company next Wednesday. In the three month period through September, its ad revenue doubled to $153.4 million. (By comparison, Facebook Inc. on Wednesday said its quarterly ad revenue totaled $2.34 billion.)

Many advertisers still view social media, particularly Twitter, as largely a free vehicle to promote their brands. Marketers have seized on the hyperactive, conversational nature of the 140-character public messaging service as a means to talk directly to consumers.

When the Superdome in New Orleans went dark during last year's Super Bowl, the Oreo cookie brand posted a tweet that said: "Power Out? No Problem." The tweet included a picture of a cookie with the line, "You can still dunk in the dark." The tweet -- shared on Twitter and Facebook more than 20,000 times -- became a marketing sensation. But it didn't translate into much revenue for Twitter.

In that Oreo vein, brands including Dannon and Toyota have tweeted up a storm in the lead up to the game and plan to tweet plenty more on Sunday, while putting most of their ad dollars elsewhere.

Other advertisers show more promise for Twitter.

Unilever says by working more closely with Twitter it is now more confident that Twitter ads work. The consumer product giant is spending more than $500,000 on its Twitter Super Bowl push, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Twitter's alliance with Axe includes buying a wide range of ads that the social-media company offers and represents a significant increase in how much the Axe brand spent on Twitter during last year's Super Bowl, according Gail Tifford, Unilever's senior director of media for North America. Ms. Tifford says it isn't enough to simply send out tweets organically. "When you look at reach of organic tweets, you are not reaching the scale you need," she said.

Adam Bain, Twitter's president of revenue, said some advertisers like to experiment with different kinds of organic content, testing out different voices and messages on Twitter. Then, once "they feel confident that a piece of content is strong" they may start paying for ad products.

Josh Nafman, senior manager of digital brand engagement for PepsiCo, which is sponsoring the Super Bowl halftime show, said Twitter employees provided guidance on what kind of content is more shareable on the platform, and helped come up with one hashtag: GBP halftime.

"I would love to say it seems like a very obvious thing but the coordination of not only hashtag strategy but also when to leverage paid media throughout that" is a difficult trick, Mr. Nafman said.

"A lot of us feel more comfortable with spending money behind" products that are "based in data and science and insights into our consumers and sports and music," said Mr. Nafman.

Twitter has developed or acquired many of its measurement and tracking tools in the past year. "Our goal is to be as strategic as we can to the marketers and their respective agencies and that means even pre-running ad campaigns before they run ad campaigns," said Twitter's Mr. Bain. "Often times, we're now in the room when they are working on the concept for the creative that is going to run on TV or on digital."

Mr. Bain declined to disclose how much revenue Twitter earns from the Super Bowl.

Bank of America, which will run a Super Bowl commercial featuring U2 performing a new single, has increased its ad spend on Twitter in general this year and said the consulting service could compel it to spend more down the road.

Still, it's hard to beat the return on investment for something that is free, namely a regular tweet.

Toyota Motor Corp. is paying for only a "handful" of promoted tweets. It will air a 60-second commercial during Super Bowl XLVIII, and use Twitter, Facebook, Instagram and Vine videos to generate buzz.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: In it for the Short Term --- The 30-year mortgage is no longer the only game in town, as wealthy borrowers are drawn to loans with repayment periods of 10, 15 or 20 years

By AnnaMaria Andriotis 
888 words
31 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Affluent borrowers are falling out of love with long-term loans.

More wealthy borrowers are ditching the 30-year mortgage in favor of shorter repayment periods. The reason: lower rates that can save borrowers thousands of dollars in interest over the life of the loan.

Lenders say much of this demand is for refinancing. Rather than restarting the clock on a new 30-year mortgage, existing borrowers may be able to keep a similar monthly payment with a lower rate on a shorter-term jumbo.

Home buyers are also interested. As mortgage rates rise, affluent borrowers face a dilemma: sign up for a costlier loan or forget financing and pay all cash for the home. The latter option means giving up attractive tax benefits; borrowers can usually deduct interest payments on up to $1 million of mortgage debt.

A quicker repayment period provides a middle-ground solution. Affluent borrowers can tap into tax benefits while paying less in interest on the mortgage. They also have the means to handle the larger monthly payments that often result from squeezing a mortgage into a shorter term.

While still high, demand for 30-year mortgages has been dropping. They accounted for 90.3% of private mortgages -- those that lenders tend to keep on their books and which are comprised mostly of jumbos -- that were originated during the first 11 months of 2013. That's slightly lower than the same period a year prior and the lowest figure in the past seven years, according to CoreLogic, a real-estate analytics firm.

Meanwhile, shorter-term mortgages have been rising: 15-year mortgages accounted for 9.1% of private mortgage originations during the first 11 months of 2013, up from 8.9% during the same period a year earlier. That is the highest level in the past seven years.

To a large extent, the move away from the 30-year loan is a rate play. When rates were at record lows, borrowers could stash the cash they didn't put into the home in other investments and earn a higher return than the interest they were paying out on their 30-year mortgage.

But as mortgage rates rise, it is harder to find those investments -- especially for borrowers who sign up for 30-year mortgages, which typically have the highest rates. Rates on mortgages with a 20-year or shorter period, while rising, can be low enough to make this arbitrage possible.

"It's a recent phenomenon," says Mike McPartland, head of investment finance for Citi Private Bank in North America. "If clients notice distinct discounts for taking a shorter product . . . and if it suits their strategy, they're going to opt into it."

Rates on 30-year fixed-rate jumbo mortgages, which exceed $417,000 in most parts of the country and $625,500 in pricier housing markets like New York, averaged 4.56% during the week ending Jan. 24, while 15-year jumbo fixed rates averaged 3.76%, according to mortgage-info website HSH.com. That spread is larger than the same week one and two years prior.

Other repayment options are popping up as well. Wells Fargo rolled out pricing for 10- and 20-year repayment periods over the past couple of years. PNC Bank says affluent borrowers have expressed interest in these loans over the past few months.

In general, with shorter-term loans, borrowers stand to get rates that are from one-quarter to more than one percentage point lower than the 30-year mortgage. The smaller the repayment period, the larger that difference.

For jumbo borrowers, such a rate break results in significant savings. For instance, at the Princeton, N.J., office of Gateway Funding Diversified Mortgage Services, a mortgage banking firm, the starting rate on a 30-year fixed-rate jumbo is 4.5%, while the starting rate on a 10-year jumbo is about 3.4%. Over the life of a $1.5 million mortgage, borrowers will pay more than $1.2 million in interest with a 30-year period, compared with roughly $271,000 in interest with a 10-year term.

Gateway Funding says roughly 5% to 10% of the jumbo mortgages it originated in its Princeton office last year had 20-year repayment periods, and another 5% to 10% had 10-year terms.

Middlesex Savings Bank, a community bank based in Natick, Mass., says 20-year mortgages accounted for about 30% of their jumbo originations last year.

Lenders say that some of this demand is coming from baby boomers who are turning to shorter repayments to pay off their mortgage before entering their golden years.

Here are a few other issues to consider:

-- Choose your term: Some banks will write almost any repayment period that a qualifying borrower requests.

-- Watch out for rates: Some banks offer the same rate to every borrower who gets a specific repayment period, regardless of their income or credit, which could result in a more creditworthy borrower paying more than he or she would elsewhere.

---

Email: AnnaMaria.Andriotis@dowjones.com

COMPANIES
Banks' ageing IT struggles to cope with rise in demand; BANKS

By Sharlene Goff and Sam Fleming 
975 words
29 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
19
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

News analysis

Growth in mobile services adds to systems' workload, write Sharlene Goff and Sam Fleming

Barely a month goes by without reports of another serious systems crash at one of the UK's biggest banks.

Last weekend's three-hour outage at Lloyds Banking Group followed a high-profile failure last month at Royal Bank of Scotland on so-called Cyber Monday, one of the busiest online shopping days ahead of Christmas.

On both occasions the banks fixed the problems quickly - and were even more keen to speedily reassure customers they would not lose money.

However consultants are warning that these problems could occur more often as banks' old IT systems struggle to cope with rapidly escalating demands from customers.

"The huge problem is that many of these systems were built 30 years ago," says Daniel Mayo of Ovum, a technology consultancy.

"The number of query-based requests has shot through the roof - banks are dealing with far greater transaction volumes than the systems were designed for."

The fast take-up of mobile banking via smartphone apps and online banking services means banks are now having to process billions of queries per year.

Lloyds, for example, says it processed 1.24bn log-ons in 2013, up 16 per cent on the previous year. It expects that figure to grow to 1.4bn this year. About half of those now come from people using mobiles and tablet computers.

That level of demand has put more pressure on to already antiquated systems , which analysts say have suffered from years of under-investment in both hardware and software.

Consultants responsible for improving banks' systems say that merely accessing some archived data has been a challenge because of the obsolete formats that are used. One says he was forced to hunt down antiquated computer equipment and put it back into service just to access data stored on old-fashioned tapes.

Also, as banks have expanded through acquisitions, they have tended to bolt new systems on to existing ones, rather than undertake the potentially more disruptive and costly process of fully integrating new customers. The result is hugely complex IT networks that may never be able to be untangled.

There were "a lot of fingers crossed as people try to get more life out of something" and a reliance on short-term fixes, says one consultant.

Figures from Celent, the research company, show that European banks have barely increased their IT spend in the past three years, despite suffering a number of serious failures. They are forecast to spend $59.7bn on IT projects this year, compared with $59.1bn in 2011. Over the same period banks in Asia are expected to increase their IT spend by 25 per cent and US banks by 16 per cent.

Jacob Jegher, research director at Celent, says there are signs that European banks are preparing to step up their IT expenditure over the next few years. But he says the growth is focused on new technology and innovation, while budgets for the more pedestrian - but essential - maintenance of existing systems are declining.

UK banks say they are spending billions of pounds on IT each year.

RBS and Lloyds, the two state-backed banks - which seem to be worst hit by IT problems - say they invest about 2bn a year.

After RBS's catastrophic IT outage in the summer of 2012 - which left millions of customers unable to access their accounts for weeks - the bank said it would spend an additional 450m over the next three years.

RBS says it has already improved its ability to recover lost customer data and introduced manual functions to enable it to override IT failures. It is also working on plans to allow small transactions - for say 20 - to go through if its servers fail.

IT specialists say banks are increasingly installing what they call "predictive analytics" - essentially software that will detect early warning signs that a system may be at risk of failing and alert the bank. This is partly in response to a warning from the regulator in 2012 to ensure their systems were not exposed to the kind of outage that caused havoc at RBS.

The Financial Conduct Authority has taken a more proactive stance on IT, warning in its "Risk Outlook" last year about the threat of interruptions to banks' IT services because of their reliance on "ageing and merged technologies and systems."

As soon as Lloyds' servers went down on Sunday night - triggered by a hardware failure - the bank sought to assure the regulator that the problem was not too serious.

But banks still have a way to go to convince regulators and customers that their systems can cope with the avalanche of new demands that are being triggered by the rapid digitalisation of banking.

Computing calamities

2012 & 2013 Unavailable funds

On multiple occasions customers of RBS Group, which includes NatWest, RBS and Ulster Bank, are left without access to their money, in some cases for days. The bank apologised, opened branches on a Sunday and waived some charges.

Aug 2013 Exchange interrupted

Goldman Sachs is potentially exposed to a $100m loss after a computer malfunction inadvertently makes a large number of erroneous options trades, disrupting trading across multiple US exchanges.

Sep 2013 Mortgage mess

Clydesdale bank is dealt a 8.9m fine by regulators after finding a software error caused repayments on variable rate mortgages to be miscalculated. Thousands underpaid their loans, but the bank had demanded that customers make up the shortfall.

Jan 2014 ATMs down

Thousands of customers at Lloyds Banking Group were unable to use debit cards for about three hours on Sunday afternoon, and at about the same time half the bank's 7,000 ATMs stopped working.

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: A Plan's Real Beneficiary

By Dawn Wotapka 
654 words
29 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

As the Affordable Care Act alters the nation's health-care landscape, business groups are closely watching to see who gets helped or hurt by the new law. In the real-estate world, one group appears to be emerging a clear winner: owners of medical-office space. Earlier this month, Harrison Street Real Estate Capital LLC paid Washington Real Estate Investment Trust more than $500 million for 22 medical-office buildings containing 1.5 million square feet of space in the Washington area. The rich purchase price translates to $320 a square foot, well above the sector's average price of $179 a square foot and close to what it would cost to replace the buildings.

The sale of Washington REIT was part of a flurry of medical-office deals in the past year. In the fourth quarter of 2013, investors spent $2.5 billion to acquire medical-office space, up 18% from a year earlier, according to Real Capital Analytics. The average price per square foot, meanwhile, climbed 13% in the same period. Buyers include REITs, private-equity firms and hedge funds.

Brokerage Newmark Grubb Knight Frank, which says it has several medical-office transactions in the pipeline totaling about half a billion dollars, expects to see its sales volume double this year from 2013. "We're seeing a voracious appetite for the product," said Todd Perman, an executive managing director of NGKF Global Healthcare Services.

Even before the Affordable Care Act became law, investors were betting that medical-office space would be a safe bet due to the large number of aging boomers who will require a high level of medical services. The demographic trends meant that medical-office space "was somewhat seen as a safe haven over the last few years," said Dan Fasulo, a managing director with Real Capital.

But the Affordable Care Act means that millions more Americans are gaining access to health care and will seek out services. These trends come as hospitals have been moving nonemergency services into office space near their campuses to reduce costs.

Supply, meanwhile, has risen modestly. After several years of little supply growth, the market added about 7.5 million square feet of medical office space last year and a similar amount should hit the market his year, according to Marcus & Millichap's Healthcare Real Estate Group. Still, oversupply currently isn't a concern because little of this new space is speculative, or built without a user in place, said Alan Pontius, the group's national director.

"New supply will not damage the supply-demand balance," he said, adding that vacancy rates should fall in 2014.

To be sure, the high prices some buyers currently are paying exposes them to risk. Medical-office space can take longer than other types of real estate to re-lease because the user base is limited and rental growth has been relatively flat in recent years.

Christopher N. Merrill, co-founder of Chicago-based Harrison Street, which has about $6 billion of assets under management, said he isn't worried about the high prices his company paid because the buildings it acquired are 90% occupied and the average lease terms are between five and 10 years. The retention rate, or tenants who re-sign leases, is between 85% and 90%.

"We think it's a very good nonvolatile investment. That's what we look for," he said. "Long term, medical office is a very attractive sector."

Mr. Merrill said his company was attracted to the wealthy patient base in and around Washington, as well as locations within walking distance of respected medical institutions including MedStar Health.

Seller Washington REIT said it decided to exit the medical office business to focus on its other businesses, which include apartments, office space and shopping centers.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Gars changes gears in equities; Fund focus

By Matthew Jeynes 
593 words
27 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
09
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Strategy is switching from dividend income to growth, says Matthew Jeynes

One of Europe's bestselling funds of recent years has made a landmark shift in its strategy for buying equities, in a sign that stock market behaviour could be set for a change.

The vast Standard Life Investments Global Absolute Return Strategies (Gars) fund contains a series of large baskets of equity investments that have long favoured companies with sustainable levels of dividend income.

But, following a change in house view at Standard Life, the equity portfolios have been switched to buying companies that show the potential to grow their earnings.

The Gars strategy, run by the fund arm of the Edinburgh-based insurance group, has rocketed in size in recent years amid bumper global sales.

The group continues to open it up to global markets and in late 2011 it was made available in the US through a partnership with John Hancock Investments, the Boston fund company. Standard Life last year also made Gars available to German retail investors, while stating that five of the six significant Canadian investment dealers had started distributing the fund.

The Gars portfolio is complex, making countless tiny bets in diverse areas, from equities and bonds to currency trading . It aims to deliver small, steady positive returns regardless of the state of underlying markets.

There are signs of sustained success and the 19.4bn UK-based version of the fund, launched in 2008, gained 3.4-17.9 per cent in each of the past five calendar years, according to FE Analytics, the data provider.

The success of the product means the equity investing shift is sure to be scrutinised by global investors eager to glean an insight into the direction of equity markets.

Andy Ford, investment specialist on the Gars team, says the focus on companies with a reliable income stream had been a key feature of the fund for several years. But as the global economic recovery continues to gather pace, and with the US Federal Reserve reducing its giant programme of economy-boosting asset purchases, companies' fundamental merits are "returning to the fore".

"We have seen significant cost-cutting in recent years, so when we do see the top line start to move, there should be significant operational leverage to kick in and gear up earnings growth," he says.

He adds: "As earnings start to improve, the situation becomes self-reinforcing as companies put cash to work in ways they have not done in recent periods."

Along with the new focus on earnings, the fund has been ramping up its European equity bets.

The team boosted its exposure to European equities throughout the second half of 2013. In its latest fact sheet, correct to the end of November, the position occupied a 6.5 per cent weighting in the fund.

"We think there could be quite a turnround for European companies. We also think that there is a bit less policy uncertainty as we move into the new year and it will definitely be beneficial that fiscal tightening has been delayed," Mr Ford says.

The team also has large positions in global equities, global real estate investment trusts (Reits) and the equity of oil majors, all geared towards finding earnings growth.

The Gars fund has retained its three other main investment themes. They focus on global growth rates; trades exploiting central bank policies around the world; and economic change in China.

Matthew Jeynes is senior reporter at Investment Adviser

MBA students turn to entrepreneurship

By Jonathan Moules 
995 words
26 January 2014
19:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

When Simo Dragicevic got round to studying for an MBA, aged 36, he appeared to be on the corporate fast track. But he quit his job as a director at Barclays Bank, which was sponsoring his studies, to become an entrepreneur, which meant he had to pay his tuition fees himself.

"I have and continue to make some large personal sacrifices," he says. "However, we can pay ourselves and invest in the business, and this is an incredibly satisfying feeling."

Dragicevic founded Bet Buddy, a supplier of user data analytics software for gaming companies, helped by his experience of implementing technology in the fields of risk, marketing and finance during his time at the bank.

He credits his decision to reinvent himself as an entrepreneur to a throwaway comment by a tutor during the first lecture of his MBA course at Cass Business School in London.

"He said, somewhat in jest and somewhat in truth, that MBAs typically don't go on to build $10bn-plus businesses," Dragicevic recalls. "However, he also said MBAs can for sure teach you the tools to build a $10m-plus business."

Although most MBA students are still unlikely to follow Dragicevic's path, he is among a growing number of MBA-educated entrepreneurs.

It is a change being driven more by the rewards of creating something of value than disillusionment with the career-enhancing potential of an MBA, says Noam Wasserman, a professor at Harvard Business School.

"Far more of our students are running to entrepreneurship, rather than running away from the non-entrepreneurial job markets," he says.

"They see entrepreneurship as a way to have much more impact, and impact much sooner, than they could in almost any other post-MBA job. They are excited about heading out into new terrain, developing ideas and businesses that fill needs that aren't yet being addressed. They are heading into that terrain with far more knowledge about what awaits them and how to avoid the recurring pitfalls that have plagued the dreams of past entrepreneurs."

The number of Harvard MBA students becoming entrepreneurs has been about 7 per cent in the past three years, Prof Wasserman says, roughly double the number before that.

Prof Wasserman runs a course for MBA students called "Founders' Dilemmas", which he describes as a "collecting ground for future founders". Twenty-eight per cent of students who have taken this course later founded companies, up from 20 per cent in 2009.

The picture is similar in China according to Shimin Chen, associate dean and MBA programme director at China Europe International Business School in Shanghai (Ceibs). "Most MBA students still look for traditional salary-based jobs with large multinational companies, consulting firms or investment banks, but because of the success of entrepreneurship in China, more and more of our students are taking it up," he says.

Like other business schools, Ceibs offers electives for its MBA students that teach skills relevant to a business founder, such as marketing and finance, and promote them as such.

The school's entrepreneurship club counts more than half of the MBA student intake among its members. Many are drawn by the positive image of entrepreneurship in Chinese society even if they themselves will never become founders, Prof Chen says.

Dragicevic found a great deal of support from his fellow students for his decision to go it alone, even though the pressure to earn back the cost of an MBA meant many did not follow his lead.

"For many the short- to medium- term financial implications and low success rate of business start-ups were not the most attractive option. For the majority who were not sponsored and having paid their MBA tuition fees, there was certainly some pressure to capitalise on the investment as early as possible."

He says his MBA tutors also taught him skills that he has found useful in running his business, which this year is expected to break even on revenues of about 350,000, as Dragicevic is still spending cash to develop operations.

"An MBA certainly provides one with tools to tackle a variety of business problems in the corporate world, and some of these tools are also valuable to the entrepreneur," he says. "I have met a few scientists and engineers who have built products but who have struggled to grasp many of the aspects of commercialising the products."

Despite support from his peers, Dragicevic feels he was discouraged by Cass from taking the path to entrepreneurship, perhaps because his potential salary increase if he had returned to Barclays would have helped the school up the UK's MBA ranking leagues.

"I was one of the top salary earners in my cohort," he says. This is something that the British ranking organisations need to address, Dragicevic believes.

Sarah Juillet, director of postgraduate careers and MBA programme at Cass, says: "While rankings are important to business schools, what is more important today is the focus on individual students and their individual ambitions. One of the ways Cass has improved this focus is the recent merger of the career service and MBA programme to align more closely on content and outcomes."

She adds that the MBA includes entrepreneurship electives, while the Peter Cullum Centre for Entrepreneurship offers incubation space, mentoring, advice and access to funding networks for early-stage start-ups. Cass alumni and students can also apply to the school's 10m venture capital fund.

"More people will take entrepreneurial paths in future, and business schools investing in entrepreneurship may not be getting the credit they deserve," Dragicevic says. "I have built a high-tech product, I am building equity in a new company, I have created jobs, I have brought business to the UK from overseas clients and I have produced original peer-reviewed research published in respected scientific journals working with British academics. But I earn a lot less than when I was at Barclays."

Personal Finance
A Student of Stupidity

By Al Lewis 
602 words
26 January 2014
05:29
The Wall Street Journal Sunday
SNJR
WSJ2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

All you need to know about multibillionaire Carl Icahn is how he describes himself on his Twitter page: "Some people get rich studying artificial intelligence. Me, I make my money studying natural stupidity."

W.C. Fields was more concise: "Never give a sucker an even break."

Mr. Icahn is 77 years old and has an estimated net worth of $20 billion, and he still loves to prey upon the stupid. Last week, Mr. Icahn was studying the natural stupidity at Apple and eBay. Those companies have been nothing but revolutionary over the past decade. They're not really all that stupid. It's just that Mr. Icahn is smarter, you see.

He wants Apple to give more of its $150 billion cash hoard to shareholders like him in the form of stock repurchase plans and higher dividends. He also wants eBay to split off PayPal as a separate company because he thinks this reorganization would result in shareholders like him getting more money.

Mr. Icahn often unveils his moves, or discusses them, on Twitter, which can sometimes drive up stock prices. You've got to admire that he is transparent, if not insulting, about his "natural stupidity" strategy.

He has purchased billions worth of Apple and eBay stock. But he's not making a bet on these companies; he's making a bet he can get these companies to do what he says.

He is smarter than the people who run them, you see, even though he has never produced much more than harassment and misery for most of his life.

In the 1980s, when Apple was bringing the world personal computers, Mr. Icahn was stripping assets away from TWA and loading the now-deceased airline up with debt.

In the 1990s, when Apple was pioneering software and opening the Apple Store, Mr. Icahn was busy with things like trying to break up U.S. Steel and RJR Nabisco.

In the 2000s, when eBay was developing online auctions and payment systems, and Apple was churning out iPods, iPads and iPhones, Mr. Icahn was doing things like feeding off the corpse of Blockbuster video.

Mr. Icahn was more accurately heralded as a corporate raider in the 1980s. Through the miracle of public relations, he is now called a shareholder activist. He says he produces shareholder value. And market pundits often cheer him on.

In the case of Apple, Mr. Icahn is attracted to the amazing cash position of one of history's most successful innovators. Like a housefly to a honey pot. Not everyone thinks giving the money to him and other investors is the greatest idea. Perhaps Apple could use these billions to revolutionize something else.

"I have no respect for Carl Icahn," said stock analyst Ronnie Moas in an interview. "He needs to start using his power in a different way."

Mr. Moas, founder of Miami-based Standpoint Research, says one reason Apple has so much cash is that it underpays its labor in Asia to build its products. He recently made news for "blacklisting" Apple in his research report making the controversial suggestion that more of Apple's cash go to the workers who make its products.

Apple has already announced a three-year plan to return $100 million to shareholders through stock buybacks and dividends. If Apple coughed up, say, $10 billion to pay workers, who knows, it might even be able to afford to create jobs in the U.S.

Nobody would even notice the money was missing.

Except Mr. Icahn. He thinks he deserves it. He's smarter.

Markets
Hedge Fund Winton Capital Plans U.S. Office; Fourth Largest European Hedge Fund by AUM Planning To Open a U.S. Office in the Summer

Ben Wright 
489 words
24 January 2014
12:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Winton Capital Management, the fourth largest European hedge fund by assets under management, is planning to open a U.S. office in the summer, according to people familiar with the matter. The move is part of a broader global expansion that could lead to the U.K.-based firm, which has nearly $25 billion in assets under management, opening offices in Japan and Australia.

Winton, in which Goldman Sach's Petershill fund owns a 9.9% stake, is one of the pioneers of managed futures strategies, which employ complex computer models to spot trends in global markets. The hedge fund employs nearly 300 people, many of them employing statistical research and big data techniques to sift enormous amounts of information.

Winton's U.S. office will be based in New York and will initially only house a handful of marketing staff, including some who will relocate from other offices. As well as in London, the firm already has offices in Oxford, Hong Kong (which opened in 2009) and Zurich (which opened in 2012).

Managed futures hedge funds have suffered poor performance in recent times partly because of a lack of clear trends in the market. This phenomenon is often attributed to the high degree of central bank intervention. The Newedge CTA index, which broadly tracks these firms, gained only 0.72% last year having fallen 2.87% in 2012 and 4.45% in 2011. However, Winton Capital's flagship $10 billion Winton Futures Funds gained 9.5% net of fees last year.

In contrast, BlueCrest Capital Management's BlueTrend fund lost 11.4% last year, while Cantab Capital Partners' flagship fund lost 27.7%, according to people with knowledge of the performance of both managed futures funds.

Additionally, the long-only Winton Global Equity Fund climbed 29.8% in 2013, outperforming the MSCI World Index which gained 27.37%. The company is understood to be focusing resources on this product, according to people familiar with the firm's plans. It wants to market it to U.S. institutional investors such as pension plans.

Of the nearly $25 billion that the firm manages, $10 billion is in the Winton Futures Fund, more than $13 billion is in separate managed accounts and $750 million is investment in the long-only equity fund.

In the last two years the firm has launched two onshore U.S. funds, which currently have a combined $300 million in assets under management. Around 40% of the assets across all Winton's products are managed for U.S. clients.

Winton's founder David Harding is understood to be one of the best paid financiers in the U.K. and ranked joint 126th in last year's Sunday Times rich list with a personal fortune estimated to be 700 million.

COMPANIES
Tech groups eye LSE main market

Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A clutch of London tech companies is set to lead a revival in the city's capital markets in 2014, with online food ordering service Just Eat preparing to go public as soon as April, writes Sally Davies .

"I've been in this market for 40 years, and it's more buoyant now than I've seen it since 1999," says Richard Holway, chairman of the TechMarketView research group. After several years of digital drought for the main London Stock Exchange, investment bankers say there are likely to be around four technology listings this year. In total, roughly 10 UK groups are thought to be considering a London float.

But some worry that the market is getting carried away. Just Eat is seeking a valuation that equates to 70 to 90 times projected 2013 earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation.

"There are some frothy ones, but the vast majority of companies in the [UK] tech scene are fairly valued," says Mr Holway.

What has changed is the attitude of institutional investors. Often criticised for conservatism, they are now showing interest in innovations in mobile, social networks, big data and cloud computing. "There is more of an understanding from main market investors that there are some very disruptive businesses that are using technology as their key differentiator," says George O'Connor of Panmure Gordon.

The LSE has been courting tech businesses for its main market. Of the 36 technology groups that made their debut last year, all but one - infrastructure software group Servelec - listed on smaller Aim.

The bourse has appointed Joanna Shields , former chief executive of public sector agency Tech City UK, and Sherry Coutu, an angel investor, to its board. It has tweaked its rules to encourage entrepreneur-led companies to join. But so far not a single group has listed under the new High Growth Segment.

The pipeline is "very strong", says Marcus Stuttard, head of UK primary markets and Aim at the LSE. As well as Just Eat, contenders include payday lender Wonga, games maker Mind Candy and cyber security group Sophos, according to bankers and analysts.

A UK listing can help a company stand out more than in the crowded US market. "Unless you're really large, you can get lost in the noise in America," says David Toms of Numis.

COMPANIES
'Animal spirits' of spending jostle for release; FT SERIES - UNTAPPED CASH; GENERAL FINANCIAL

By Ed Crooks 
1,187 words
24 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
15
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

News analysis

Growing demand and balance sheets make the US the top candidate for a pick-up in investment, writes Ed Crooks

US chief executives are running out of excuses for not raising capital spending more quickly. Repeated political confrontations over taxes and government borrowing and spending; nervousness about when the Federal Reserve would start to throttle back on quantitative easing; the deepening crisis in the eurozone: these were all compelling reasons for chief executives to be "sitting on the sidelines", as Andrew Liveris of Dow Chemical put it last month.

Today those issues are fading away. Last month, the Fed announced that it would start buying Treasury bonds at a slower rate. This month, Congress agreed a broad spending deal for the first time since 2009, although a possible clash over the US government's debt limit is still a lingering concern. Indicators of financial stress in the eurozone, including government bond yields for Greece, Ireland and Spain, have eased significantly.

Chief executives' statements and companies' guidance on capital spending plans for the coming year suggest they are still cautious. Business confidence is rising, but the manifestation of that confidence in physical investment is still patchy. However, if investment is going to accelerate in any developed economy, the US is the most plausible candidate.

Demand is rising and balance sheets are strong. With every passing year the scars of the 2007-09 crisis heal a little more, and old equipment is more urgently in need of replacement. Some analysts believe that 2014 will be the year that capital spending finally begins to take off . "We feel very strongly about it," says Nick Nelson, global equity strategist at UBS. "We feel an upturn in capital spending is ripe to happen now."

If it does not, it will look like a sign that the barriers to business investment are more fundamental than had been thought.

Expectations for capital spending this year vary widely both between sectors, and between companies in the same sector. General Electric, the US industrial group, is heavily exposed to capital spending as a manufacturer of equipment from aero engines to power generators, and talked this month about a "generally improving" environment. Revenues at its industrial operations were up 6 per cent in the fourth quarter of last year compared with the equivalent period of 2012, while its orders were up 8 per cent, with a 25 per cent rise in China, an 8 per cent gain in the US and even a 3 per cent uptick in Europe.

However, Jeff Bornstein, GE's chief financial officer, refused to get carried away. "There's a lot of sentiment that the US is about to turn the corner, that potentially the economy will pick up more broadly, including capital goods," he said last week. "But at some level that remains to be seen."

GE expects its own spending on plant, equipment and software to be about the same this year as the $4bn it spent in 2013.

Commercial aerospace, which is booming globally, is one of the industries where strong demand is encouraging growth in capital spending.

Greg Hayes, chief financial officer of United Technologies, which makes aircraft electronics and aero engines, said this week it expected capital spending to be $2bn this year, up from $1.7bn in 2013. While that would put "some pressure" on cash flows, he said, "these are critical investments for our commercial aerospace business as we prepare for an unprecedented ramp-up in production".

Boeing, which has seen a surge of orders for its commercial aircraft, has been stepping up investment already. It said last October it expected to spend $2.3bn in 2013, up from $1.7bn in both 2011 and 2012.

Oil and gas production is another industry where output is booming and capital spending continues to rise. But ExxonMobil and Chevron, the two largest US oil companies by market capitalisation, have been under pressure from investors to curb the rise in their capital spending budgets. Chevron last month said it expected its capital and exploration spending to drop from $42bn last year to $39.8bn in 2014. John Watson, chief executive, said he expected 2013 to have been "a relative peak year for investments".

Smaller companies benefiting from US shale oil are still going great guns, however. Continental Resources, the largest producer in the Bakken shale of North Dakota, is planning a 13 per cent increase this year. Overall, capital budgets in US oil and gas production are set to rise by 8.5 per cent this year, according to James West, an analyst at Barclays: more than double last year's 4 per cent.

Rising supplies of shale oil and gas are also stimulating investment in the refining and chemicals industries that use them as feedstocks. Phillips 66, the refining, chemicals and pipelines group, expects a 24 per cent increase in capital spending this year compared with its plan for last year.

In other industries, however, the case for investment is harder to make. The big four US telecommunications groups - AT&T, Verizon Communications, Sprint and a resurgent T-Mobile US, are among the biggest spenders in the world because of investments in 4G wireless networks and - for AT&T and Verizon in particular - advanced television and internet services.

However, the initial rollout of wireless broadband networks based on LTE technology is largely complete, and while capital spending is unlikely to fall very far because operators need to spend heavily to maintain their services, it is unlikely to rise. AT&T expects to spend about $20bn on capex this year, down from the $21bn it is estimated to have spent in 2013.

The outlook in technology is similarly mixed. Microsoft is expecting to spend $6.5bn in the year to June, about 50 per cent more than last year, as it increases investment in data centres. Intel, however, said earlier this month that it expected capital spending of about $11bn this year, roughly the same as in 2012 and 2013. The optimists believe this caution is excessive, and that as the year progresses we will see more companies prepared to commit to increased investment.

Mark Zandi, chief economist of Moody'sAnalytics, believes the US could be on the verge of a significant pick-up in business investment. Borrowing from John Maynard Keynes, he argues that the crucial factor is the "animal spirits" of corporate executives, boards and investors.

"Each year that passes since the recession without things going badly wrong, the more likely it is that those spirits will get unleashed," he says.

However, he does entertain one remaining threat: the risk of " secular stagnation " highlighted by Lawrence Summers, the former US Treasury secretary. The idea that the world is heading for a long period of sluggish growth does not seem likely, Mr Zandi says. The longer we go without an acceleration in capital spending, though, the more plausible it will seem.

Additional reporting by Robert Wright, Paul Taylor and Neil Munshi

WORKING LIVES
The risks and rewards of a newly legal high

By Emma Jacobs 
1,264 words
24 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The legalisation of cannabis in some US states has been a boon for a venture capital firm that focuses on the marijuana market

Three years ago Brendan Kennedy sat his parents down to tell them of his career change.After spending almost half a million dollars on his education (a degree in architecture at Berkeley, a masters in engineering from Washington University and an MBA from Yale Management School), his mother and father - both in their eighties - were baffled by his decision to quit his well-paid job providing valuations of Silicon Valley technology companies to set up a venture capital firm that invests in the cannabis industry.

Despite living in San Francisco, Mr Kennedy's parents missed out on the hippy era. They were too busy bringing up their seven children and holding down jobs (his father was a science teacher, his mother a relationships counsellor). They could not see the appeal to their son of an industry that was then largely illegal.

Yet Mr Kennedy was convinced. After years in Silicon Valley, researching and analysing the financial value of nascent industries, he felt that the legitimate cannabis business, like electric cars and the social media companies he had seen in their embryonic stages ("it felt like having a crystal ball to see into the future"), would also prove to be profitable and ultimately mainstream.

After discussing his idea with two peers (one from school, another from Yale) who ultimately became his business partners, he decided to start researching the idea of setting up a venture capital firm to invest in legal cannabis businesses, both medical and recreational, while he was still employed at SVB Analytics.

"I had access to every industry database you could imagine and could get no information on the market. There was no research. I started to read dry legal reports." It was "real boots-on-the-ground research", he says. Attendees at academic and cannabis business conferences gave him a wide berth, believing that with his smart suit, athletic build and short hair, he worked for drug enforcement agencies. "I certainly met a cast of characters," he laughs.

Reflecting on the business proposals he has received, he says: "Most propositions are awful." The companies he came across were "immature", the entrepreneurs behind them were "unprofessional". A few asked him to inspect their illegal crops hidden away on farms. Others kept wads of cash. Many people approached him with the same idea, thinking they were the first to have it. "I know more about cannabis-infused chocolate than I ever thought I would," he reflects.

The 41-year-old spent a great deal of time figuring out the legal position, given that federal law says possession, manufacture and sale is illegal but states vary in their approach.

More than three years later, the Seattle resident, whose company Privateer Holdings has raised $7m in investments and hopes to raise up to $50m more this year, believes his convictions have been vindicated. Cannabis went on sale in stores in Colorado at the beginning of this year, while Washington state is expected to allow sales later this year after voting to legalise the use and possession of cannabis for people aged over 21. The two states are among 20 to have approved marijuana for medical use. The legal market in the US is predicted to reach $2.3bn this year, according to ArcView Group, a cannabis investor network. It calculates that the market will be worth $10.2bn by 2018.

Initially, it was difficult getting investment. The founders all had backgrounds in banking, so drew on their networks and found the best way to meet potential investors was through "blind introductions", meaning that the people they met were only told they would find the business opportunity interesting but not what it was. "We surprised a few people, but no one kicked us out," he says. Interested investors generally opted to hold back until they knew if the legalisation initiatives in Washington and Colorado would be passed. "Once those initiatives passed in November 2012, it was much easier - investors started coming to us."

They deliberately kept their ambitions for funds modest because it was so hard to find sound investments. "We couldn't trust them [cannabis entrepreneurs]. They had worked illegally for years. It doesn't make them bad business people."

So far, Privateer has bought three companies: Leafly, an online ratings site for dispensaries and marijuana strains; Lafitte Ventures, a Canadian operation growing medical marijuana; and Arbormain, which will lease warehousing for legal cannabis businesses.

In all the Privateer companies' marketing materials there are no depictions of cannabis leaves, no joints and "definitely no Grateful Dead", he says. "We believe that by investing in smart, professional brands we can hasten change. None of us are hippies, most people who consume cannabis aren't hippies."

Mr Kennedy says a lot of people are in the industry because "they want access to the product and culture". He insists that he is not interested and had not touched cannabis since the age of 19. However, when he started the business he decided he should reacquaint himself with the product. "I tried a few indicas and sativas [two types of cannabis plant] - I felt I needed to see the difference."

It has been hard to find savvy business people willing to take the risk and work in this new and occasionally murky industry. "It's not like we could bring a successful CEO in from a public company." A lot of his effort has gone into building teams and "hand-holding to get things done professionally and legally".

Mr Kennedy recently brought in a former drugs enforcement officer to work on compliance. "It is a maze even for people who know about the legislation," he says. Banks have been cautious about getting involved with companies, fearing they might be charged under drugs racketeering or money laundering rules.

Jeffrey Miron, senior lecturer at Harvard's economics department and author of Drug War Crimes: The Consequences of Prohibition , believes many businesses are wary because the existing laws in these states have restrictions and no one knows "how long federal tolerance will last". He adds: "A new president, only three years away, can reimpose federal intervention. So rational businesses are still wary of legalisation because it's not quite real legalisation."

While Mr Kennedy is concerned with the return on investment, he is also in the business because he strongly believes in the social case for legalisation. "The damage done by the drug laws is worse than the drug itself. The war on drugs leads to high incarceration levels and cannabis prohibition perpetuates race discrimination in the country."

The "social return" as well as the "capital return", he says, is what unites his investors, who range from "left-leaning progressives to right-leaning libertarians who would disagree on every topic except for this". Most prefer to be anonymous. His investors are "motivated" but perhaps, most importantly, patient.

Kris Kane, managing director of 4Front advisers, which advises high-end retailers offering medical marijuana, says the risk attached to the legal status of cannabis means those seeing it as a purely rational business investment are likely to be scared off. Yet like any high-risk business, the rewards could be high if you are able to lay the foundations and become a market leader.

Mr Kennedy says he and his co-founders feel like "agents of change", adding: "We know cannabis prohibition will end in our lifetime."
