Auto Lender's Drive Reaps a Fortune

By Andrew R. Johnson and Telis Demos 
1,034 words
23 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Thomas Dundon's 13,556-square-foot Dallas home features eight bedrooms, indoor and outdoor tennis courts, three wet bars, a swimming pool and an indoor slide that goes from the second floor to the first.

Those are only some of the spoils Mr. Dundon, 42 years old, has collected during his journey to the top of the subprime auto-lending business, where lenders make loans to borrowers with blemished credit. In 2012 alone he raked in $85.2 million in total compensation.

Now Mr. Dundon is poised to become a subprime billionaire.

Santander Consumer USA Holdings Inc., the Dallas-based auto-lending and consumer-finance company run by Mr. Dundon, is slated to debut as a publicly traded company Thursday. Owners of the firm, a unit of Spanish lender Banco Santander SA, raised $1.8 billion by selling about 75 million shares, or 22% of its outstanding common stock, at $24 a share. The deal values the company at about $8.3 billion, not including the exercising of stock options.

Mr. Dundon, the chief executive, stands to see his stake valued at $1.2 billion, based on the 13.6% share of the company that he will hold after the initial public offering, including stock options.

"It would not have crossed anyone's mind that this was possible," Mr. Dundon said about the company's success in an interview Wednesday. "I'm not sure if we did it over again that you could replicate the success."

The windfall is notable in an industry still dogged by the memories of the financial crisis. Subprime mortgages were among the first to go bust when the housing market foundered, erasing billions of dollars in assets for lenders, the Wall Street firms that packaged their loans into securities and the investors who bought them. Subprime mortgage lending activity has shriveled to just a tiny fraction of its peak during the housing boom.

But auto loans are a different breed. Subprime auto loans weren't hit by the huge losses seen in other lending segments. Industry experts say many borrowers continued to make auto-loan payments before they paid down other types of loans, including mortgages and credit cards, because they rely on their vehicles to get to work.

Mr. Dundon keeps a high profile in sports-crazed Dallas. He attended the wedding of Dallas Cowboys quarterback Tony Romo in 2011, according to local news reports, and has played in golf tournaments with professional golfer Hunter Mahan.

But Mr. Dundon's friends and acquaintances paint a picture of a self-made entrepreneur who came from humble beginnings. He earned his fortune by remaining intensely focused on achieving the goals he set for himself and his business partners, and by refusing to dwell on setbacks, according to those who know him.

"He's hyper-focused," said Mark Molthan, a Dallas real-estate developer who built Mr. Dundon's home and has known him since high school. "When he knows what he wants . . . he figures out a way to get it."

Mr. Dundon attended high school in Plano, near Dallas, and was the president of the Phi Gamma Delta fraternity at Southern Methodist University.

Former fraternity members recall Mr. Dundon's leadership skills following a fire that destroyed Phi Gamma Delta's house in 1992. Jon Altschuler, a commercial real-estate executive in Dallas who was in Phi Gamma Delta at the time, said Mr. Dundon led the effort to find replacement housing for the fraternity while continuing to recruit new members. He also was instrumental in marshaling support from the local business community to raise funds to support the fraternity's operations.

After graduating in 1993 with a degree in economics, Mr. Dundon started a burger restaurant in Fort Worth, Texas, called Izzy's. The venture lasted less than a year, according to friends. He often joked that Izzy's became "Wasy's," they said.

"He just moved on," said Mr. Altschuler. "He's never been a down-in-the-dumps kind of guy."

After the burger joint failed, Mr. Dundon got his start in the auto business, winning a job in the finance department of a Dallas auto dealer. He and some co-workers in 1995 struck out on their own to form an auto-finance company whose rapid success attracted outside investors. Banco Santander in 2006 agreed to buy a 90% stake in the company for $650 million. In 2011, Santander sold part of its stake to private-equity firms Warburg Pincus LLC, Kohlberg Kravis Roberts & Co. and Centerbridge Partners LP, which together owned about 24% of the company before the IPO.

For the first nine months of 2013, the company earned $581.7 million, or $1.69 a share, down from $611.7 million, or $1.72 a share, a year earlier.

The IPO comes amid a boom in auto lending, which has been a rare bright spot for lenders facing tepid customer demand for other types of credit. Loans to borrowers with weak credit have been particularly strong. Auto-loan originations to subprime borrowers -- defined as consumers with scores below 660 on the industry's FICO measure -- totaled $153 billion last year, up from $66 billion in 2009, according to Moody'sAnalytics and Equifax.

Other large consumer-finance companies are considering going public, as well. Ally Financial Inc., the former finance arm of General Motors Co., is looking to launch an IPO after the Federal Reserve releases results of its annual "stress tests" of big banks, people familiar with the matter have said. General Electric Co., meanwhile, wants to spin off its consumer-finance arm, the company has said.

Despite Mr. Dundon's success, friends say he hasn't let his ego swell and remains focused on his family. He and his wife, Veruschka, have four children, and they often allow their kids' sports teams and others to use the athletic facilities on their property, according to Mr. Dundon and his friends.

"It's definitely a kid's wonderland," Mr. Molthan said.

---

Lisa Schwartz and Matt Jarzemsky contributed to this article.

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: Big Bet on a 'Small World'

By Dawn Wotapka 
743 words
22 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Corrections & Amplifications

MetLife Inc. purchased a share of the Westin New York at Times Square. The Deal of the Week column in the Property Report on Wednesday incorrectly said that MetLife purchased a share of the Westin New York Grand Central.

(WSJ Jan. 23, 2014)

(END)

A few years ago, hotels and resorts were among the least attractive sectors in commercial real estate. Properties were filing for bankruptcy at a rapid rate, lenders were writing down the value of mortgages and buyers were scarce. These days, hotels and resorts are hot properties.

That is good news for investors that were big buyers during the downturn, including Blackstone Group and Rockpoint Group. The rebound also has worked out well for insurance giant MetLife Inc., which added to its hotel portfolio during the downturn.

Now MetLife is doubling down. In late December, MetLife bought a stake in four brand-name hotels, including a majority stake in two resorts -- the Swan and Dolphin hotels -- at the Walt Disney World Resort in Lake Buena Vista, Fla. The company declined to disclose what it paid, but the two adjacent properties recently were appraised at $638 million, according to Morningstar.

MetLife, which has a $12 billion real-estate portfolio, said it expects to do more hotel deals. "Investing in hotels and resort properties fits with our strategy to be in robust, tier-one markets in order to drive income and build appreciation," said Robert Merck, a senior managing director at MetLife who heads the global real-estate investment group.

The deal comes as investors are showing more interest in resort hotels nationwide. Last year, investors spent $4.5 billion to acquire resort hotels, up 140% from 2012. The average price per unit increased 112% to nearly $367,000, according to preliminary year-end research from Real Capital Analytics. Both were the highest levels since 2007.

Resorts suffered a double whammy during the downturn: Vacation spending dried up while businesses scaled back on the conferences that can be lucrative for hotel operators. "It was the perfect storm," said Dan Fasulo, a Real Capital Analytics managing director. As the lending environment deteriorated, many hotels fell into foreclosure and were sold at significant discounts.

When the economy began to recover, acquisitions of apartment buildings and office space were first to rebound. Now resorts are "finally starting to get some traction in this recovery," said Mr. Fasulo.

Resort hotels are benefiting as businesses and consumers spend more. In November, revenue per available room was $78.14, up 8% from the prior year, according STR, a hotel research firm. Occupancy climbed to 57.8%, from 56.4% a year earlier.

What's more, research from MLV & Co., which tracks publicly held hotels, shows that convention-center bookings are up 5% for 2014 after being down 1% at this time in 2013. "You're seeing leisure accelerate across resorts [and] investors can see the group bookings on the books," said MLV analyst Ryan Meliker. "You've got more confidence and willingness to pay up for those assets."

Industry watchers expect resort deals to continue to be hot in 2014. "Resorts in general, and Florida resorts, are viewed as highly attractive," said Dan Peek, who leads brokerage HFF's hotel practice. "The market has come around to the point that most resort markets are worthy investments."

The MetLife deal involved MetLife buying Silverpeak Real Estate Partners' 25% interest in several properties owned by Tishman Realty Corp., including the Westin New York Grand Central and the Sheraton Chicago Hotel & Towers. MetLife already owned a portion of the Swan and Dolphin hotels, so this deal brings its ownership to 59%.

The resort complex, which opened in 1989 and 1990 and has about 2,300 rooms, is located between Epcot and Disney's Hollywood Studios. Disney doesn't own the buildings, but it owns the underlying land and receives compensation. Westin runs the Swan while Sheraton operates the Dolphin.

The Swan's and Dolphin's occupancy rates were above 80% in 2013, up from the high 60s in 2002, according to Morningstar. "The properties perform much better relative to their peer groups," Morningstar wrote. The firm calculated the revenue per available room at more than $139 and the annual net cash flow at $39 million. It values each room at $211,217.

Thailand Likely to Cut Rates as Crisis Persists; Economists Forecast Another Quarter-Percentage-Point Cut to 2.25% Amid Political Unrest

Nopparat Chaichalearmmongkol 
780 words
21 January 2014
03:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

BANGKOK Thailand's central bank is expected to cut interest rates at its meeting Wednesday as political unrest continues to engulf the exporter of automobiles and electronics.

Almost daily antigovernment protests, many of them violent, have destabilized the country since late last year. Prime Minister Yingluck Shinawatra has called elections for Feb. 2 but the opposition says they will boycott the polls, meaning a likely protracted battle.

At the Bank of Thailand's most recent meeting, as political protests started to gather steam in November, the bank cut rates by 0.25 percentage point to 2.25%.

Policy makers are likely to follow that up Wednesday with another cut of 0.25 percentage point, said Rahul Bajoria, an economist with Barclays in Singapore, one of a number of economists expecting a cut. Consumer prices rose only 1.67% in December from the year-ago period, and global oil prices are falling, which means the bank has room to cut rates without sparking inflation, Mr. Bajoria said.

"It would send out a message the central bank wants to support growth," he said.

Even before the instability, the outlook for Thailand's economy was shaky. Exports, which account for around two-thirds of the economy, have performed poorly, declining 4.1% on the year in November, the latest month for which data are available.

The automobile industry is suffering because of weak demand in other Asian markets. Exports from the nation's electronics industry, which supplies parts for personal computers but not the fast-growing smartphone market also have been disappointing.

The turmoil is taking its toll on the economy. Tourism, which accounts for 7% of national output, has been hard hit as foreign travelers postpone journeys. Plans to build multibillion-dollar infrastructure, including high-speed rail lines, look likely to face delays amid the political gridlock.

The Finance Ministry last week slashed its growth forecast for 2014 to 3.1%, compared with an earlier projection of 3.5% to 4.0%. Failure to push ahead this year with the 2.2 trillion baht ($66.6 billion) infrastructure plan could push growth as low as 2%, the ministry estimated.

Tim Condon, an economist with ING in Singapore, also says the central bank needs to convince the market that rates will stay low until growth picks ups.

"An aggressive rate cut accompanied by a statement saying that growth has fallen well below the BoT's forecast, and rates will remain low to reverse this state of affairs, would lead me to up my GDP growth forecast," Mr. Condon said.

ING is forecasting growth at 3% for 2014, lower than Thailand's trend rate of growth of 4.5%. Mr. Condon expects a 0.25 percentage point cut Wednesday. Others expect further cuts in the near future.

"The recent political impasse poses significant downside risks to growth, which could prompt further rate cuts over the coming months as the central bank looks to support the domestic economy," said Fred Gibson, an economist at Moody'sAnalytics.

Such monetary easing, though, might have little direct effect in the current environment. The previous rate cut failed to filter through into higher bank lending because Thai banks are currently trying to reduce debt exposure.

Thai household debt stands at 80% of gross domestic product, one of the highest ratios in Asia, reflecting years of aggressive lending to finance house purchases and auto loans. A government tax rebate two years ago for first-time car owners also helped boost debt levels.

"Lowering borrowing costs would not be able to provide much boost to either consumption or investment given the damped confidence and household leverage," said Kobsidthi Silpachai, head of capital markets research at Kasikornbank, a Thai bank. He said he is expecting the bank to hold rates steady.

Others say inflation could become an issue down the road if monetary policy is kept easy, pushing investors to pull out of Thai stocks and bonds. That could put further pressure on the Thai baht, which already is trading at a three-year low of 32.86 to the U.S. dollar.

Usara Wilaipich, an economist with Standard Chartered in Thailand, says the currency weakness could become inflationary. She said she expects the central bank to maintain its policy rate Wednesday.

"Given that Thailand is a net oil importer, any further baht weakening due to portfolio outflows or additional rate cuts would add to inflationary pressure," she said. "Policy makers need to balance the potential growth benefits of a rate cut against the potential inflationary impact."

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
US regulators 'funded at a level to fail'; News analysis; Budgets

By Joe Morris 
692 words
20 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The SEC and CFTC are falling further behind industry in technology investment, finds Joe Morris

Even with new budget increases, US securities regulators are falling further behind the market when it comes to technology investment, and the investment advisers and swaps dealers they oversee are likely to remain largely uninspected.

The first comprehensive federal spending plan since 2009, hammered out in Congress last week, raises the Securities and Exchange Commission funding by $29m, to $1.35bn, or $324m below the White House's request . The Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) budget rises about $10m, to $215m, or $100m less than requested .

The additional funding sought by the administration for the SEC was intended to pay in part for the hiring of 250 examiners dedicated to registered investment advisers, whom the regulator now examines only once every 11 years on average.

"Whatever increase in the examination of advisers they are able to perform will have to be gained through efficiencies, and while I am certain that examiners' productivity can be increased, I don't think we can expect to see a significant change in the overall examination rate," says Neil Simon, vice-president for government relations at the Investment Adviser Association.

The CFTC will be able to reinstate a number of staff positions eliminated during across-the-board budget cuts last year. However, it too was hoping to add examiners for newly registered swaps dealers, execution facilities and clearing houses - one of the commission's new responsibilities under the Dodd-Frank financial reform law.

"To an enormous extent, they are going to be dependent on the industry self-regulatory organisations to provide that oversight," says Barbara Roper, director of investor protection for the Consumer Federation of America.

"The SEC situation is bad, but the CFTC situation is criminal."

The spending plan, meanwhile, cuts in half a $50m "reserve fund" the SEC has used for investing in its technology infrastructure. This includes the "consolidated audit trail", a multi-billion-dollar trade-tracking system that became a priority at the commission following the 2010 flash crash.

Financial services companies are, by contrast, maintaining their technology spending at about 1.5 per cent of revenue, according to research from IPC Systems, the developer of trading communications systems.

"The SEC and CFTC have a tremendous challenge ahead of them, both in terms of having the investment dollars needed for technology and for people," says Bart Bartolozzi, senior product marketing manager at IPC.

"Both of them want to build these large data repositories and push all the data that they have pulled from investigations into it and run analytics against it," he says. "Now, they have to react to an issue that has happened; they do not have much of a historical context to say this is something that this firm has historically done that we need to check."

Dennis Kelleher, president of Better Markets, the financial reform advocacy group, contends the technology allocations suggest the SEC and CFTC are "funded at a level to fail".

"What both agencies are trying to do is get a better understanding of high-speed computer trading and how it is affecting markets, and yet they are not getting anywhere near the ability to do that," Mr Kelleher says. "When you cut the reserve fund at the SEC by 50 per cent, and on top of that you underfund them overall, you really are putting them in an impossible position."

Asset management companies tacitly acknowledge their distrust of market oversight through their gradual migration to dark pools and internalisers, or trading arrangements with brokers in which orders are matched within the brokers' own companies, Mr Kelleher says.

Indeed, Rosenblatt Securities estimates that more than 37 per cent of all US stock trades are now executed at off-exchange venues run by brokers.

"Asset managers and investors and traders are fleeing the exchanges to try and find a place where they are not the victims of predatory conduct," Mr Kelleher says.

"They are in internalisers and dark pools so that they can get fair execution."

M&A Firm Knows All, Even What You Drink

By Dana Mattioli 
1,027 words
17 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
18
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

RICHMOND, Va. -- All investment banks say they focus on clients, but Harris Williams & Co. takes the practice to a new level.

The merger-and-acquisition specialist finds out all it can about executives at companies willing to buy or sell. Among details it gathers: languages they speak, their favorite cocktail and whether they are more window shoppers than reliable bidders.

Harris Williams, based here, is attempting to usher in data-mining techniques that have worked in other industries to answer a perennial Wall Street question: Will a deal be consummated?

For decades, deal makers have relied on personal relationships and connections to get deals done. But Harris Williams maintains that its focus on "predictive analytics" -- collecting and analyzing hundreds of thousands of data points in one main computer system shared by the firm -- helps it strike deals and minimize time wasted on talks that go nowhere.

Closing deals is every banker's goal because firms receive the bulk of their fees after a sale.

"We know that this knowledge leads to fewer failed deals," said Harris Williams co-founder and managing director Hiter Harris. The bank focuses on deals valued at around $1.5 billion or below involving smaller and midsize companies.

In November, the bank used its data to help guide the sale of automotive-collision-repair company Caliber Collision Centers to Omers Private Equity.

When compiling the initial list of potential buyers, Harris Williams looked at several variables such as whether a company had ever pursued an acquisition in this industry, said co-founder and managing director Chris Williams. When the time came for submitting bids, more than two-thirds of the group Harris invited submitted final offers, all of which were priced at or above where the bank expected them to be, according to Mr. Williams, who said the analytics helped generate that result.

Retailers and tech companies have long embraced predictive analytics, or collecting and crunching reams of data to anticipate future sales preferences.

The effort requires applying algorithms to the data to predict likely future outcomes. It is standard for companies from travel-booking website Orbitz Worldwide Inc. to Amazon.com Inc.

But the techniques are unlikely to gain wide acceptance among deal makers anytime soon. Asked about predictive analytics, some bankers said they don't want to bother inputting information, even if their bank supplies basic call-log software.

"I don't have time for that," said one veteran banker, who added that he would rather spend time meeting with clients than recording what they say.

Plus, sharing isn't exactly part of the sharp-elbowed culture of investment banking, where bonuses can turn on who receives credit for deals within a bank. Some bankers want to keep details of their client relationships private, even from colleagues.

Harris Williams's database was the brainchild of Mr. Harris and Mr. Williams, Harvard Business School classmates.

Mr. Harris, a math major and a self-described nerd, is a compulsive list maker and taskmaster. Across his desk, legal pads carry to-do lists. His Christmas shopping was done in October.

Not long after they started the company in 1991, both men took copious notes on clients, mostly private-equity firms. Back then, rudimentary tracking software sufficed because there weren't that many private-equity firms.

But in the past 30 years, the number of private-equity firms in North America has ballooned, making what Harris Williams bankers do a lot more complicated. In 1984, there were about 67 private-equity firms world-wide, according to data provider Preqin. Today, that number is 5,330.

So, Harris began meticulously chronicling all of its interactions with executives at private-equity firms and corporations. as well as their strategic goals and track record in deals. Its bankers were instructed to write down notes from every phone call, email, dinner and keep tabs on material from quantitative information, like how often a deal maker or firm dropped out of a sales auction before the final round, to personal details, like what years someone ran a marathon.

The thinking was that keeping close to a few dozen clients was relatively easy, but when that group grew into the thousands, they needed a more methodical approach, according to Mr. Harris.

"At the beginning it was pretty primitive," Mr. Harris said. But the important flagpole the bank planted was cultural, he said, especially as the firm itself grew. The bank employs more than 150 bankers across eight offices internationally.

The database has logic built into it, in the form of algorithms, that automatically generate data points bankers can use to predict behavior of potential buyers and sellers. Database training happens as soon as new hires join the firm, he said. The company established early on that if a banker knew something, it had to go in the database.

No one outside the firm has access to the database, including publicly traded PNC Financial Services Group Inc., which bought Harris Williams in 2005.

The data "can make all the difference in the world when you're picking between three companies who all appear to like the business you're selling," said Jay Jester, managing director on the private-equity team from Audax Group. Mr. Jester said he has tapped Harris Williams to help buy and sell a number of companies.

When Harris Williams was trying to help an oil-field-services company sell itself several years ago, executive managing director Ned Valentine persuaded his client's board to meet with a company that submitted a relatively low first bid. Based on the bank's 15 years of data on how this bidder acted in sales processes, Mr. Valentine predicted that the company had a tendency to raise its final bid significantly from its initial offer. So the board agreed to meet with the company, Mr. Valentine recalls. Ultimately, the potential buyer increased its offer by more than 50% and bought the company, he said.

"There is a real, selfish motive to doing this. We want to win," Mr. Williams said.

Time Warner Sells Manhattan Office Space to Related for $1.3 Billion; Related Cos. to Lease Back Space Until 2019

Everdeen Mason

Time Warner Inc. confirmed Thursday it sold its office space at the Time Warner Center in Manhattan for $1.3 billion to developer Related Cos. and will lease back the office space until 2019.

Related and Time Warner were close to a deal but needed financing for the bid, The Wall Street Journal reported earlier this week. Two sovereign-wealth funds the Government of Singapore Investment Corp. and the Abu Dhabi Investment Authority have aided Related in purchasing the 1.1 million square feet of office space.

As part of the deal, Time Warner will relocate to a building that Related plans to develop in its Hudson Yards project on Manhattan's far West Side. Time Warner said it would acquire more than one million square feet of available commercial space in 30 Hudson Yards located at the southwest corner of 10th Avenue and 33rd Street.

The company expects about 5,000 employees from its corporate operations and its HBO, Turner Broadcasting, and Warner Bros. businesses to occupy the Hudson Yards space by the end of 2018.

Time Warner and Related have agreed to develop Hudson Yards and expect to finish the final building development documents in the first half of the year. Related hasn't completed financing for that building.

Time Warner spent about $520 million to construct its current headquarters about a decade ago. Chief Executive Jeff Bewkes said the move is an important step in the company's two-year long process to start consolidating its New York operations.

Thursday's deal marks one of the biggest in a surge of deals involving foreign investors in U.S. commercial real estate. Increased demand from overseas has helped drive U.S. commercial property values to record levels, particularly in major cities favored by global firms.

Foreign buyers are seeking out the U.S. as a place to spend their growing piles of cash, particularly as other markets such as Europe remain anemic. Non-U.S. buyers purchased more than $46 billion of property in 2013, up from $35 billion in 2012 and the most since 2007, according to real estate data firm RealCapital Analytics Inc.

Wednesday's Markets
S&P 500 Nabs Another Record

By Chris Dieterich 
577 words
16 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A two-day rally in stocks carried the S&P 500 to an all-time high, as Bank of America and Apple fueled outsize gains in financial and technology shares.

The S&P 500 index advanced 9.50 points, or 0.5%, to close at 1848.38, topping the previous high of 1848.36 on Dec. 31.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 108.08 points, or 0.7%, to 16481.94. The Nasdaq Composite Index climbed 31.87 points, or 0.8%, to 4214.88, ending at its highest level in more than 13 years.

After the S&P 500's 30% climb in 2013, a sluggish start to 2014 left some investors scratching their heads. A surprisingly weak December jobs report last Friday spread more doubt and on Monday, the S&P 500 suffered its biggest one-day decline in months.

But stocks bounced back Tuesday after a strong round of retail-sales data sparked a rally. Optimism carried into Wednesday as upbeat corporate news mixed with positive global economic reports. The S&P 500 is up 1.6% over the past two sessions, erasing all early-year declines.

"The market wasn't ready to crack," said Michael Antonelli, an equity sales trader at Robert W. Baird & Co. "Clients have remained steadfast in their equities."

Technology shares were among the biggest gainers. Apple advanced $10.97, or 2%, to $557.36 after Chief Executive Tim Cook said the company's recent agreement with China Mobile helped drive record iPhone sales in China in the first quarter.

Bank of America rallied 38 cents, or 2.3%, to 17.15 after the banking giant reported fourth-quarter earnings and revenue that exceeded analyst estimates amid improving credit quality.

"There's a little bit of enthusiasm reversal," said Dan Greenhaus, chief global strategist at brokerage firm BTIG. "We feel earnings are going to be OK."

Kristina Hooper, U.S. investment strategist at Allianz Global Investors, which has $409 billion in assets under management, said the market is likely to be swayed by each big data point in the weeks ahead. Still, she expects stocks to grind higher over the course of this year. "We're going to continue to be data driven, and as a result the market is likely to get more volatile," she said.

The yield on the 10-year Treasury note ticked up to 2.884% from 2.869% late Tuesday.

February crude-oil futures advanced 1.7% to settle at $94.17 a barrel, while January gold futures gave up 0.6% to settle at $1,238.10 a troy ounce. The dollar gained some ground against both the euro and the yen.

The Stoxx Europe 600 climbed 1%, ending with its highest close since January 2008.

The World Bank late Tuesday raised its 2014 global growth forecast by 0.2 percentage point to 3.2%. It also upgraded its growth outlook for the euro zone, helping offset data showing German economic growth slowed to 0.4% in 2013.

Asian shares moved higher early Thursday. Japan's Nikkei was up 0.6% amid a softer yen. Hong Kong's Hang Seng index was up 0.4%, while the Shanghai Composite Index was up 0.1% and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was up 0.7%.

Payday lenders club together to create loans database

By Elaine Moore 
479 words
15 January 2014
18:22
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

After repeatedly saying that it was impossible to create a database for payday loans the UK's largest payday lenders are clubbing together to set one up.

Wonga, Quick Quid, Mr Lender and ThinkFinance have all signed up to provide information to a real time credit-reporting system run by Callcredit, one of the country's largest credit reference agencies.

Lenders who participate in the payday loan database will be able to improve affordability checks by seeing whether a potential client has recently taken out a loan with another provider, and whether or not that loan has been repaid.

The U-turn comes as the industry attempts to convince the government and regulator that it is a responsible division of the UK's financial services industry after coming under attack for poor lending practises.

In November, the Treasury announced plans to set a cap on the amount that short-term lenders could charge borrowers. In December, the Department for Business, Innovation and Skills declared that this move would not go far enough and recommended that providers should be forced to share information about their clients in a more effective way through a real time database.

In the US, a number of states, including Florida and Oklahoma have already introduced statewide databases for payday lenders. Last year, a group of payday lenders in Alabama filed a lawsuit against the state in an attempt to stop the creation of its database.

Callcredit said that payday lenders would not be the only beneficiaries of the new system and that mainstream lenders had also shown interest. The database will be open for testing in April and operational by mid-May. Callcredit does not plan to charge users more to upload information to the daily database than it does for its existing monthly system.

"The existing systems in which providers upload information monthly were designed for traditional mainstream lending, such as mortgages, which occur on a less frequent basis," said Peter Mansfield, managing director of Callcredit.

"The lending landscape has changed, so introducing daily reporting will give a clearer insight into a borrower's credit history."

Online payday lenders typically provide customers with access to small sums of money for less than 30 days at a high cost. The fees charged can be equivalent to annual interest rates of more than 6,000 per cent.

As the UK's payday loans industry has undergone rapid growth its detractors have grown more voluble, accusing companies of irresponsible lending and pushing vulnerable borrowers into serious financial difficulty.

Wonga, which has more than 1m customers, insists that the company already uses thousands of pieces of information for every loan decision. "Wonga is a business built on the intelligent use of quality data to make accurate and responsible lending decisions," said head of decision analytics, Rael Sarembock.

Markets
Fed Eyes Banks' Commodity Activities; Some Say Companies Use Their Foothold in Market to Influence Supply Flows and Prices; Risk Also an Issue

Ryan Tracy 
819 words
14 January 2014
18:09
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON The Federal Reserve took a small step toward developing potential new restrictions on banks' activities in physical-commodity markets such as aluminum and oil, but it stopped short of introducing new rules.

The Fed, which has been studying the issue for at least two years, said it wants to examine "all aspects" of bank-owned commodity businesses, including "potential conflicts of interest" at the banks and the risks that commodity subsidiaries might pose to financial stability. The regulator said it will give banks and other parties two months to submit input on how it should proceed before considering "what further action, including a rulemaking, is warranted."

The Fed's review comes amid growing controversy over claims that banks are using holdings such as metals warehouses, power plants and oil barges to influence the flow of commodities, as well as prices. Major industrial consumers of aluminum, such as Miller-Coors LLC, have complained that bank-owned metals warehouses have driven up prices and wait times to obtain supplies. A Miller representative didn't respond to requests for comment.

The Fed said it is considering several measures that could limit banks' ability or desire to own such businesses, including imposing additional capital requirements and capping the amount of and length of time banks can own such assets and the revenue they can derive from those activities.

Some banks are already preparing for new and potentially tougher restrictions. J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. announced in July it would sell its physical commodity units and has narrowed its field of bidders for the assets to a final handful of parties. Morgan Stanley announced the sale in December of its division that stores, trades and moves physical oil and refined products, as well as its equity stake in Heidmar Holdings LLC, which manages pools of oil tankers. And Goldman Sachs Group Inc. has entertained offers for certain units, such as its Metro International Trade Services group of metal warehouses.

The Fed noted that the 1999 Gramm-Leach-Bliley law allowed banks who were trading in commodities before October 1997 to continue doing so as long as those businesses accounted for less than 5% of their assets, a provision that grandfathered in commodity businesses at Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley. The regulator asked whether those firms should face additional capital requirements or other restrictions to ensure activities at those firms "do not pose undue risks."

Spokesmen for Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley and J.P. Morgan declined to comment on the Fed move.

The Fed said it was reviewing its policy given concerns that many of the firms it previously authorized to trade in such markets could be put at risk by their commodity businesses, such as delivery of physical assets like oil or aluminum. It cited recent disasters like the 2010 Deepwater Horizon oil spill in the Gulf of Mexico, the Fukushima Daiichi nuclear-plant meltdown in Japan and the fatal Pacific Gas & Electric Co. natural-gas pipeline explosion in San Bruno, Calif., in 2010 as examples of risks that could potentially destabilize the financial system if a bank were involved and subject to legal action.

The Fed's move disappointed politicians and consumer advocates who were expecting more forceful action. The central bank said last July it would reconsider policies that led it to grant waivers allowing banks to enter physical commodity markets and own related assets.

Sen. Sherrod Brown (D. Ohio), who plans a hearing on the issue Wednesday, said in a statement the Fed move was "overdue and insufficient." Senior officials from the Fed, the Commodity Futures Trading Commission and the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission are to testify at the afternoon hearing. The Fed official, banking-supervision director Michael Gibson, is expected to testify that the Fed could also prohibit banks from holding certain physical commodities that pose undue risks.

Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy-analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc., said the industry had been expecting the Fed to be more specific about a potential capital surcharge related to commodities, but the documents released by the Fed on Tuesday demonstrate an "an extremely cautious approach."

"A tried-and-true D.C. evasion tactic is to, just before being reprimanded on the Hill, call for a study or set up a task force. We see it here in its unchecked glory with a Fed study that should have commenced many months ago," said outgoing CFTC Commissioner Bart Chilton.

People familiar with the Fed's policy-making process said officials struggled with how broad and aggressive any new restrictions should be and what legal authority the central bank should use to pursue changes. Banks have been operating in physical commodity markets under differing exemptions and permissions for decades in some cases.

Scottish Mortgage is UK trust king; Fund focus

By Matthew Jeynes 
520 words
13 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The investment trust is banking on biotech, writes Matthew Jeynes

The freshly crowned king of the UK investment trust industry, Scottish Mortgage, is banking on a series of new bets in the biotechnology sector as it tries to retain its leading position in 2014.

Late last year, Baillie Gifford's James Anderson, the manager of the trust, saw it eclipse the Alliance Trust to become the biggest trust listed in London, reaching a market capitalisation of 3bn.

Established in 1909, Scottish Mortgage has been helped by its global investment outlook. It gained 204.1 per cent in the past five years, compared with an 81.8 per cent gain from UK-focused arch rival Alliance Trust, according to FE Analytics, the data provider.

It has also benefited from longstanding bets on innovators such as Amazon and Google, the US internet groups.

Mr Anderson says areas such as healthcare, transport and energy would be the next to profit from technological innovation. Healthcare will be "the most important area" for innovation, he says, thanks to advances in biotechnology including the sequencing of the human genome.

The developments are both "fascinating and exciting" because they have the potential to "destroy so much market capital and tradition", he says.

The manager cites Illumina, the US-based gene sequencing group, now the fifth-largest holding in the trust, as a company at the forefront of these advances.

Illumina manufactures equipment used to analyse gene structures. It has been the subject of bid speculation in recent years, with US pharmaceutical heavyweight Roche making a failed approach in 2012.

Mr Anderson, along with deputy manager Tom Slater, has added a number of other biotechnology companies in recent months.

These include Genomic Health, a cancer research specialist, and Myriad Genetics, a molecular diagnostic company.

Mr Slater says it is impossible to know if the new companies will be the winners of the future but "the opportunity is so large that even partial success could be extremely lucrative".

Scottish Mortgage remains smaller than Alliance Trust in terms of its total assets, but it is bigger than its rival in terms of market capitalisation.

The trust's shares are currently trading at a slight premium to its net asset value, while Alliance Trust's shares are trading at a 12.3 per cent discount, according to data from the Association of Investment Companies.

Scottish Mortgage's shares reached a premium only recently, following a surge in demand in 2013. The managers have indicated they might issue new shares to satisfy the demand and prevent the premium hitting precarious levels.

In running the fund, Mr Anderson continues to focus on long-term investment with two main themes: the rise of China and the potential of new and disruptive technology.

In the past year, the managers also invested in Twitter, the microblogging service, during its initial public offering, as well as Tesla Motors, the electric car company founded by Elon Musk that has grown significantly in the past year.

Matthew Jeynes is senior reporter at Investment Adviser

IBM Creates Unit, Fund to Expand Watson's Reach

By Spencer E. Ante 
713 words
10 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
19
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

International Business Machines Corp. is creating a business unit for its Watson supercomputer and starting a $100 million venture-capital fund to spur more apps built on the technology, a bid to accelerate the promising system's slow transformation into a big business.

The venture fund is part of a more-than-$1 billion investment IBM will make into the new Watson unit, which IBM plans to grow to more than 2,000 people, from several hundred today, the company said. Many of the new hires will be salespeople and consultants to help clients devise ways to use Watson.

"We are now moving into more of a rapid-expansion phase," said Michael Rhodin, an IBM senior vice president who will lead the unit.

The announcement comes as IBM is having difficulty turning Watson into a salable line of software three years after the machine defeated two "Jeopardy" champions on TV. The Wall Street Journal earlier this week reported that Watson's revenue is far from the company's ambitious targets.

IBM Chief Executive Virginia "Ginni" Rometty has said she hopes Watson will generate $10 billion in annual revenue within 10 years, according to the transcript of an October 2013 conference call reviewed by the Journal. She set that target after the executive in charge of Watson projected it would bring in $1 billion of revenue a year by 2018. Watson had recorded total revenue of less than $100 million as of late October, according to the transcript.

The company won't disclose financial information for the Watson unit, which Mr. Rhodin said would remain part of IBM's business analytics software portfolio. IBM last year projected the analytics unit would grow to $20 billion in revenue by 2015, up from a previous $16 billion target.

IBM is looking to revive growth after six straight quarters of revenue declines. Company executives, led by Ms. Rometty, see Watson's smart software as a valuable tool to answer tough problems. The company is building versions of Watson that can comb through studies and patient records to determine the optimal treatment for a particular cancer, or examine a customer's portfolio and recommend an investment strategy.

But the software can take years to develop, and doesn't work with SoftLayer Technologies Inc., the cloud-computing provider IBM recently bought for $2 billion. Moreover, IBM has yet to figure out a business model for Watson. IBM may license the software or charge companies a fee to access a series of digital Watson "advisers." It is also exploring ways to share in a client's results, such as new revenue or cost savings.

"We've made incredible progress," said Mr. Rhodin. "There is lots more to do."

As part of Thursday's announcement, IBM said it is rolling out two new digital Watson advisers: one helps companies draw insights from mountains of digital data, and the other creates graphical visualizations of data. The company said it would also launch a "Digital Life" service online so consumers can experiment with Watson to, for example, build an app that suggests recipes based on a person's dietary restrictions.

Another effort, begun last fall, allows independent software developers to write Watson "apps," much like those for smartphones and tablets. IBM might take a cut of the developers' revenue, much as Apple Inc. does with its app store.

More than 750 people and businesses have contacted IBM about working with Watson, the company said.

IBM is assessing the proposals. The venture fund could attract startups to the Watson ecosystem and help them build their businesses faster, said Mr. Rhodin.

Fluid Inc., a San Francisco e-commerce consultant and software developer, hopes to build an app using Watson that would let consumers ask questions on a retailer's website, as they would with a sales associate in a store.

Fluid CEO Kent Deverell says the app could help people to more easily find the products they need and prompt more sales. But Fluid still needs to finish the app, and find retailers to use it.

"We would not be pursuing it if we did not think it had big commercial potential," he said.

The Wall Street Journal Online

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Being a boomerang boss paid off for Dan Glaser.

He rejoined the executive team of Marsh Inc. 15 years after he left, shaking up management and showing improvement for the struggling insurance brokerage within months of taking charge. During his second time around, he says, "I very much understood the culture of the institution."

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 165 by 249, depth 1
pict091.jpg

Early last year, Mr. Glaser was rewarded with a promotion to chief executive of parent company Marsh & McLennan Cos. "His ascension largely reflected his successful turnaround of its biggest unit," recalls David Nadler, the recently retired vice chairman of the professional-services firm.

Many executives are making similar return trips these days, signaling an end to the negative stigma that was once attached to employment U-turns. They bring new skills, a different perspective and appreciation of their old employer without having to learn the ropes. But a comeback can also cause resentment and hurt morale. Smart planning may help you to avoid possible pitfalls and smooth your re-entry.

Among the U.S. business that have recruited former executives since 2012 are Procter and Gamble Co., J.C. Penney Co., PepsiCo Inc., Nike Inc., and Toys "R" Us Inc. Returnees are showing up outside the C-suite, too. A "http://online.wsj.com/news/articles/SB10001424052702304355104579236003135253082" in The Wall Street Journal noted that many businesses, facing a shortage of skilled workers, are eager to take back proven staffers.

And it isn't just the corporate sector: This month, William Bratton embarked on his second stint as New York Police Department Commissioner, a job he left in 1996.

Successful boomerangs typically depart on good terms and keep ties with key former associates. That was true for Mr. Glaser, who got rehired after he headed the U.K. and Irish operation of American International Group Inc., an insurance and financial-services firm. The first people Mr. Glaser pursued for his Marsh management team were two fellow alumni. "I convinced both of them to boomerang with me," he says.

Likewise for Alex Gorsky. He advanced to CEO of Johnson & Johnson in 2012, four years after he returned from rival health-care giant Novartis SA. He says he stayed in touch with CEO William Weldon and other J&J executives during his four-year absence. "I knew that I had broad support if I returned," he says. "That made the decision even easier."

It also helps to "return with humility," Mr. Gorsky recommends. He recollects calling every leader of his medical-device unit within days of rejoining J&J and telling them "that I was happy to be back." He was supervising prior peers, but "worked hard to not be resented," a person familiar with the matter remembers.

Larry Thompson acted humbly in a different way when he resumed the role of Pepsi's general counsel and corporate secretary in July 2012. He considered himself a newcomer even though he had been gone only 14 months.

"You are starting a new job as a boomerang,'' Mr. Thompson explains. "You need to partner with your colleagues. So, he didn't automatically reject changes that occurred within his Pepsi department while he taught law school. Some boomerang executives arrogantly insist that "the way things were done while I was here were absolutely the best way," he adds.

Neri Bukspan, an Ernst & Young LLP partner who returned last May after 11 years as chief accountant at Standard & Poor's, won acceptance from his current colleagues partly by slowing his usual rapid pace and delving deeper into their activities. He got the go-slow idea from his executive coach at the major accounting firm.

He concedes it can be uncomfortable for a returnee to step back rather than hit the ground running. "You think you know the old environment. But the environment has changed. You're different."

Certain executives flourish upon re-entry because they demonstrate useful expertise gained at other workplaces. Mr. Gorsky, for instance, says he recognized the potential benefits of centralized management during his Novartis stint. Named a J&J vice chairman in December 2010, he took control of a new manufacturing and supply group created to make sure all plants met quality standards. He then began to centralize and standardize supply chain and quality systems.

Matt Spielman returned to Return Path Inc. last April after working for three other businesses since 2002. The chief marketing officer of the marketing analytics firm frequently advises salespeople there about how to pitch senior marketers based on his six years selling advertising for a Viacom Inc. unit.

"I am able to walk in the shoes of our clients," Mr. Spielman observes. "Salespeople have said that my insights helped them see prospective clients in a different way."

Mr. Spielman managed to avoid a common pitfall. "You should not be superior and arrogant about all the things you learned" while you were away, cautions Ben Dattner, a New York executive coach.

Perhaps the most important advice of all? A boomerang executive who achieves results quickly "neuters the ability of others to be critical," says Ruth Porat, a senior investment banker rehired by Morgan Stanley & Co. in 1996 after three years at Smith Barney Inc. She did just that. In 2009, Ms. Porat became Morgan's chief financial officer and the most powerful woman on Wall Street.

Market Report: Dow Gives Up Ground

By Alexandra Scaggs

Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. stock benchmarks found little direction Wednesday as investors looked ahead to the afternoon release of the Federal Reserve's latest meeting minutes.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 55 points, or 0.3%, to 16476 in midday trade. The S&P 500-stock index edged up one point, or 0.1%, to 1839, and the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 17 points, or 0.4%, to 4170.

Stocks have retreated from 2013's closing high in the first trading sessions of the year, despite a handful of stronger-than-expected economic reports.

Traders and strategists said that buyers found little reason to step in on Wednesday ahead of the release of the Fed's minutes from its latest meeting, when officials announced a reduction of its easy-money bond-purchase program. The minutes were due at 2 p.m. EST.

"It stands to reason that stocks weren't going to shoot out of the blocks at the same trajectory as we saw equities rise in the fourth quarter of last year," said Mark Luschini, chief investment strategist at Janney Montgomery Scott. "That pace was unsustainable."

In economic news, Automatic Data Processing and Moody'sAnalytics said December job growth in the private sector increased to 238,000 from 215,000 in November. It was expected to slip to 200,000. The ADP report is seen as a preview of the U.S. government's job report, due Friday.

The report "is absolutely fantastic," said Colin Cieszynski, senior markets analyst with brokerage CMC Markets. "It looks like the U.S. economy is getting a lot of traction."

The news followed data Tuesday showing the trade deficit narrowed more than expected in November.

Earlier, the Mortgage Bankers Association said mortgage applications rose 2.6% on a seasonally adjusted basis in the latest week. A slight decline in purchase applications was offset by gains in refinancing activity.

In corporate news, Micron Technology rose 9.1% after the semiconductor maker reported fiscal first-quarter earnings and revenue that were well above analyst estimates, boosted by the recent acquisition of Elpida Memory in the last year. The stock more than tripled in 2013 and was the second-best performer among S&P 500 components.

J.C. Penney shares dropped 8.1% after the company said it was "pleased" with its holiday season results, but didn't offer any sales figures in its statement.

Constellation Brands rallied 8.3% after the wine, beer and spirits company beat Wall Street's estimates for its fiscal third-quarter earnings and raised its projections for its yearly profits.

The Container Store Group slumped 14% after the company reported fiscal third-quarter earnings that topped forecasts, but provided a full-year earnings and revenue outlook below current forecasts. The results were the first since the company went public in November.

Ford Motor Chief Executive Officer Alan Mulally said Tuesday he won't leave the auto maker to take the CEO job at Microsoft when current CEO Steve Ballmer retires. Ford shares gained 1.6%, while Microsoft shares slipped 1.6%.

In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 index rose 0.1% to 329.75, its highest close since May 19, 2008.

Portugal's PSI 20 jumped 1.4% to 7055.27, led by a 2.7% rise for Banco Comercial Portugues.

November retail sales in the euro zone jumped 1.4% on the month, the fastest pace seen in 12 years, and well above expectations of a 0.1% rise. In addition, German manufacturing orders climbed 2.1% in November, topping forecasts for a 1.5% increase. Germany's trade surplus also increased more than expected.

Air France-KLM rose 7.3% after the airline reported a rise in passenger traffic of 2.1% and a load factor rise of 0.9 points.

Asian Stocks

Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rallied 1.9% to 16121.45, the first gain of 2014. The gains came as the dollar topped 105 yen, helping some exporters benefit from a weaker currency. Among them were Suzuki Motor, which gained 3%, while TDK added 2.4%.

There was a sharp move on the ChiNext, China's board for startup companies, which jumped 3.9% to 1405.02 after regulators gave insurers permission to buy shares on the board. The ChiNext was a rare ray of light in mainland China last year, surging 82.7%.

The Shanghai Composite declined 0.2% to 2044.34.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Quarterly Analysis --- Exchange-Traded Funds: Key Trends in a Milestone Year for ETFs --- As the oldest ETF turned 20, the industry saw new Fidelity funds and more money run by 'strategists'

By Ari I. Weinberg 
1,080 words
6 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

It was a heady year for ETFs.

The year began with the 20th anniversary of SPDR S&P 500, the first and still the largest ETF in the U.S., and throughout 2013 investors kept up their voracious appetite for exchange-traded funds. Investors pumped a net $188.5 billion into U.S. ETFs and related exchange-traded products, according to IndexUniverse LLC. That topped 2012's record of $188.4 billion, despite heavy withdrawals from SPDR Gold Shares as the price of gold tumbled.

Here's a look at six key developments and issues in the ETF realm in 2013:

Fidelity Makes a Move

Fidelity Investments introduced 10 low-cost sector funds, a decade after launching what had been its only ETF, the Fidelity Nasdaq Composite Index ETF. The move expands its existing sector-fund business (of actively managed mutual funds) and challenges rival State Street Corp.'s popular line of Select Sector SPDRs. The Fidelity funds are based on MSCI indexes, with day-to-day management by a unit of BlackRock Inc. Yes, the same BlackRock that issues the market-leading iShares ETFs.

"It's a big and interesting partnership," says Matt Hougan, president of ETF analytics for IndexUniverse. "But it's not yet the game-changer that people were expecting." Each of the 10 funds has annual expenses of 0.12%, marginally lower than competing sector funds, but investor interest has been scant. Launched in late October, the funds hold $329 million in assets. That much sometimes moves in (or out) of several Select Sector funds in a day.

Shaking Up Index Funds

Even as the boring old market-capitalization-weighted S&P 500 soared 29.6% in 2013, investors rushed into exchange-traded funds and notes with alternative approaches to stock indexing.

As issuers and index firms debate the proper terminology for these approaches (smart beta, strategic beta, factor indexing, etc.), investors poured a net $42 billion into more than 400 such funds, bringing their assets to $155 billion, according to IndexUniverse. Varieties include products that equally weight their holdings, that pick stocks based on factors such as company earnings and dividend growth, and that focus on lower-volatility stocks.

Proponents say these strategies allow for more precise and calculated risk-taking. Opponents say such calculations themselves belie "pure" indexing. "The debate has more noise than substance," says Larry Petrone, research director for consulting firm kasina LLC, "but there is some evidence that combining factor-based strategies with traditional indexing can build a better risk-return profile."

Rise of the Strategists

An emerging class of asset manager continued to gather steam in 2013. Known collectively as ETF strategists, these investment advisers manage money using one or more strategies in which at least 50% of the holdings are in ETFs. As of September, portfolios tracked by Morningstar Inc. totaled $86 billion, up 37% in a year.

The largest ETF strategist is Windhaven Asset Management, a unit of Charles Schwab Corp., which managed $18.2 billion. "Tactical" and "hybrid" strategies, in which the managers expressly look to trim and trade their portfolios as markets move, account for 87% of the assets tracked by Morningstar.

An ETF strategist may be brought in by a financial adviser who previously called the shots on an investor's portfolio. In that case, clients "will want to know how the adviser's role compares with the role of the new asset manager, and how they'll learn about changes in the portfolio," says Morningstar analyst Andrew Gogerty.

Investors opting for an ETF strategist alongside their own adviser should be mindful of fees charged by each.

Vanguard's Big Gains

While BlackRock's iShares unit and State Street command the most U.S. ETF assets -- $662 billion and $392 billion, respectively -- the greatest beneficiary of the post-financial-crisis rush to low-cost, passive ETF investing has been Vanguard Group. In 2013, investors moved a net $55 billion into Vanguard ETFs, according to IndexUniverse, compared with $40 billion into iShares funds and $14 billion to State Street offerings. Vanguard's share of the ETF market has grown to 19.6% from 18.1% in a year, with $334 billion in ETF assets.

Active Management Lags

While some large bond ETFs are actively managed, active stock picking continues to be rare in ETFs. " 'Next year' has been the year for active equity every year since 2005," says Mr. Hougan of IndexUniverse.

At year's end, only $14.7 billion in assets were spread across 68 actively managed ETFs, with 50% of that split almost evenly between two Pimco fixed-income funds, according to XTF Inc.

The holy grail -- and potential watershed moment for active stock ETFs -- is said to be the regulatory blessing of nontransparent active ETFs. Unlike most other ETFs, these would disclose their holdings only quarterly -- and that means active managers wouldn't face concerns about front running and tipping off others in the marketplace.

Several fund families have applied to the SEC for the ability to offer such nontransparent actively managed ETFs. While chatter and filings picked up in 2013, it'll be "big news if it happens," says Mr. Hougan. Even then, it may take a few years for such funds to get a toehold.

Debating the 'Right' Price

When interest-rate anxiety led bond prices to tumble in May and June, some bond ETFs began trading at consistent discounts to their reported net asset value.

That created grumbling about perceived problems with the products, which are intended to trade at prices very close to the value of their underlying holdings. But it also highlighted a fundamental question to which there isn't a certain answer: When thinly traded securities such as some municipal bonds are packaged in easily traded ETFs, is the more accurate value the one calculated for the bonds (often without them actually trading) or the value reflected in the trading of the ETF shares?

"More and more, ETFs are becoming the true market," Mark Wiedman, global head of iShares, wrote in a letter to iShares investors at the end of June.

---

Mr. Weinberg is a writer in New York. Email him at reports@wsj.com.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Western Asset Management suffers torrid times in emerging markets; Fund focus

By Matthew Jeynes 
550 words
6 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Sovereign debt investments are vulnerable, finds Matthew Jeynes

Western Asset Management, Legg Mason's giant bond fund subsidiary, has suffered torrid times in terms of its Global Multi Strategy Bond funds, as emerging market debt investments went into the red.

The Global Multi Strategy Bond funds, one based in Ireland and another in the UK, are designed to offer non-US investors access to California-based Western Asset's vast range of fixed income strategies in a single-fund format.

The Dublin-based version has $1.5bn in assets under management while the UK version has reached $1bn, making them two of the most significant products of their kind.

However, in spite of strong performance during the 2008 credit crisis, the funds' returns have been lacklustre over the past three years. The UK-based fund has had a three-year gain of 7.7 per cent compared with an average return from funds in the IMA's Global Bond sector of 8.8 per cent, according to data from FE Analytics, ranking it in the third quartile.

In the past year it lost 3 per cent, while funds in the wider sector lost 1.2 per cent.

The fund's ultra-aggressive 35 per cent weighting in emerging market debt, as well as volatile high-yield bond holdings, were sold down by the markets when it emerged the US Federal Reserve was calling time on its "easy money" policies of the past few years.

Ian Edmonds, who heads the London-based team that manages the fund, says they have been shifting out of emerging market corporate bonds and into the markets' government bonds instead.

"Recently, corporate bonds have outperformed sovereigns, so I have started to switch back into sovereign issuers in dollar terms," he says.

"I have been investing in countries such as South Africa and Hungary, where you can get 5 or 6 per cent yield on bonds that are 10 to 12 years in maturity."

The team has also closed racier currency positions, Mr Edmonds says, and is selling investment-grade bonds in developed markets as part of the wider switch into emerging market government bonds .

The net effect of the changes is that, while the overall risk levels in the portfolios should be lower, the emerging market debt weighting will remain largely unchanged.

Denise Collins, investment manager at City Asset Management, says the investment house has a "relatively negative" outlook for emerging market debt.

"The asset class looks cheaper, but sovereign debt will probably continue to underperform, assuming US Treasuries continue to reprice," she says.

"Shorter-maturity bonds should mitigate interest-rate volatility, and diversification is key, but the asset class remains vulnerable."

Adrian Lowcock, senior investment manager at Hargreaves Lansdown, the fund broker, says emerging market debt could start to look "interesting" if prices continue to fall.

"The best way to access emerging market debt for most investors is through a global bond fund, where the manager can tactically allocate to emerging markets," he says.

"It is hard to know when will be the right time to buy in. No one knows where the bottom of the market will be."

Matthew Jeynes is senior reporter at Investment Adviser

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Pension funds beat actuarial targets

By Steve Johnson 
342 words
6 January 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
02
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Rebounding equity markets mean most UK pension funds are likely to have outperformed their actuarial assumptions over the past decade, according to State Street Investment Analytics.

Separately, US pension funds sponsored by S&P 1500 companies saw their funding ratios surge to 95 per cent at the end of December, according to Mercer, the consultancy, up from 74 per cent in December 2012 and the highest year-end figure since 2007.

State Street estimated that the average UK pension fund generated an investment return of 10 per cent in 2013. Charity funds, which typically have a greater exposure to "real" assets such as equities, thanks to their longer time horizons, will have done better still, returning 13 per cent.

As a result, the typical pension fund will have returned around 10 per cent a year over the past five years and 8 per cent per annum during the past decade, "comfortably exceeding most actuaries' assumptions for asset growth", said Jeanette Patrizio, senior vice-president of SSIA.

In 2012, the UK's Financial Services Authority told pension providers to reduce their midpoint projection for long-term returns from 7 per cent to just 5 per cent, in line with the forecasts of most European and Asian funds.

US funds are still factoring in long-term returns of 8 per cent a year. Many commentators believe this has allowed public plans in particular to sidestep painful reforms .

However, Mercer found the collective deficit of US private sector S&P 1500 schemes has fallen to $103bn, from $557bn a year ago.

The improvement was driven by a combination of strong equity returns and a rise in corporate bond yields, which allows schemes to discount their liabilities at a faster rate.

Despite these factors being replicated in the UK, Mercer said the accounting deficits of FTSE 350 schemes actually rose from 72bn to 97bn (a funding ratio of 85 per cent) last year, due to an increase in long-term inflation expectations.
