Management
Companies Need to Be More Aware of Risk; Research Shows Real Dangers to Companies Are at Middle-Management Level

Eugene Burke 
1,627 words
5 January 2014
08:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The year now ending has already seen its fair share of corporate scandals, sending shudders and shock waves through stock markets. From pharmaceutical bribery to the horse-meat scandal, phone hacking and recent oil spills, it is clear that no industry is safe from disrepute which raises the question, which of your employees will pose the biggest risk to your organization?

The financial sector has undoubtedly faced the greatest scrutiny and reforming the industry continues to be a key priority for governments across Europe. New regulation forcing banks to restructure their operations places increasing pressure on changing the "high stakes" culture of the trading floor.

But while many businesses are focusing on modifying regulatory policies and procedures, many are being blindsided by not assessing the people aspect of risk. People's actions what they do or fail to do can pose a huge risk to a business, which can impact share prices, break laws and catalyze industry reform.

But how do we define behavioral risk and how can we measure for it?

Employees, whether they are at senior, middle or front-line level, all possess certain risky traits that may cause harm to a company. What can be measured to assess behavioral risk is people's individual and collective resilience and appetite for risk by analyzing eight behaviors.

Resilience to risk is about assessing people's decision-making capabilities and how effective their communication skills are. The riskiest leaders and managers will misjudge the impact a certain decision could have on the organization; they'll restrict who they consult before making the decision and are likely to gloss over crucial data. Communication-wise, riskier managers will be poorer at articulating those decisions and setting an appropriate tone for others to follow. At the front line, people will ignore procedures, have an inability to accept individual or shared responsibility, or just focus on "getting things done," without due care and attention.

The responsibility for many recent scandals has often been placed at the feet of senior leaders and CEOs. But it isn't just decision-making at the top that should come under scrutiny; actually it is their failing to address people's actions further down the chain.

By not addressing the root cause, businesses are self-perpetuating risk. Whilst risk cannot be eliminated, it has an upside and a downside and organizations need to be aware of their own appetite for and resilience to risk so it can be managed in a constructive way.

Exclusive data for The Wall Street Journal Europe taken from CEB's SHL Talent Analytics database measures how risky 20,000 employees are across a range of industry sectors in Europe. It paints a distinctly different picture of where the greatest behavioral risk lies across an organization and it isn't at the C-suite level.

The data reveals that behavioral risk is, in fact, lower at more senior levels: showing that one-in-21 executive leaders across Europe are risky, (which is considerably lower than the global average of one in 15 at this level).

This means there is a lower proportion of senior executives who are likely to create risk in the organization. This is what you would hope to see, given the seniority of decision-making at board level.

European senior managers possess slightly higher levels of risk at one in 14. As organizations depend upon senior leaders to account for and mitigate risk, finding managers that exhibit lower levels of behavioral risk is crucial.

There is, however, is still behavioral risk at the top, which means organizations need to examine just how effective their senior leaders are when managing this element. For example, are they failing to listen to good advice from others? And are they keeping the overall company strategy in mind when making important decisions for the business?

Furthermore, behavioral risk increases significantly as you continue down through the corporate hierarchy. We've calculated that one in eight middle managers in European businesses display risky behavioral traits, which usually include poor decision-making and weak communication.

The number of middle managers in corporate hierarchies amplified this risk, further increasing the chances of a corporate scandal. The data also reveals that one in five individual contributors (or front-line staff) possess similar behaviors to their middle-manager counterparts, which is a matter of concern for product assurance and customer service.

From our data we found that there is an execution gap at the middle-manager level. Yet there are the employees with a critical role to play as the interface between strategy set at the top and operational execution. Managing compliance issues and instilling commitment in front-line staff, whilst communicating decisions from leaders in a relevant way so staff feel motivated, is no easy task. So why is the risk at this level so high?

It could be that employers have sought to retain talent by promoting employees with strong technical backgrounds into middle management positions. Lack of investment in training and development has resulted in a generation of managers who aren't equipped with the critical skills necessary to effectively communicate and manage people and therefore pose a significant risk to that particular organization. It is, in fact, vital that these middle managers are equipped with the right skill set and given appropriate support to be successful in their role. If these issues aren't addressed, a subsequent error or ongoing compliance issue could damage a company's reputation or profit margin in some cases irreparably.

While European executives are transforming their business to adapt to new markets, aligning talent to meet these changes is a major challenge. The board needs to ask themselves whether they have the right talent in place at the middle management level and whether they've invested enough in them. Equipping board members with this information is crucial to support rapid change, and will help them to adjust their talent pipelines to support the businesses objectives.

Companies should also look at how they are empowering staff to raise concerns if they are to improve their approach to risk. Enforcing ethical standards and having an effective channel by which frontliners and middle managers feel comfortable communicating issues is essential. This will help to boost compliance and commitment from employees when their views are being noticed. Too often, whistleblowers are ignored, or even demonized, but senior managers need to listen more to viewpoints from across the business to pick up and act on vital clues that will help them manage risk more effectively.

CEB also analyzed risk across 17 industries identifying which ones are the most risky in Europe. It found that there is a one in five chance of attracting and employing someone with a high level of behavioral risk at every level in both the engineering and oil & gas sectors. This is worrying, given the potential environmental damage at stake here.

Industries such as banking and health care also attract a higher than average level of risk among employees, with a one in six chance. This comes as no surprise given the recent stories in the U.K. around mistreatment of patients and the financial product mis-selling epidemic. It is clear that these sectors are less likely to be taking behavioral risk into account when they attract and hire people due to the hard technical skills needed to carry out the job. To be an effective middle manager, however, softer skills such as listening and consulting are vital to managing and motivating a team and CEB's data show that these sectors are really lacking in these.

On the other hand, professional services and retail average only one in eight employees with high behavioral risk. Perhaps because these industries are client- and customer-focused, softer people skills are crucial. Effective performance in a job isn't just about what you know, but also how you manage a team to meet overall business goals.

If we are to learn from the so-called toxic company cultures portrayed in the media, organizations must view risk holistically, considering policies, procedures and people together. To avoid the corporate blind-spot, the C-suite needs to have a better understanding of how their employees behave, something that should be an important part of a human resources departments work. The board needs this insight to take a view on a company's level of resilience and the appetite for risk across the business.

Some level of risk is to be expected; what is important is how the organization manages it. Recent events, particularly in the banking sector, have focused on the negative consequences of not managing risk effectively, and quite rightly so.

But organizations need to know that there can be a positive impact from risk. It is about striking the right balance between creating risk to seize on new markets whilst being resilient.

The technology sector is a good example of this, where the market is so competitive that companies are constantly taking risks and innovating to make the next leap forward. Nevertheless, organizations need to be aware of the boundaries between achieving a competitive advantage without finding themselves in their next corporate crisis.

While many businesses are waking up to the need for broader understanding of the people they employ, most are at an early stage of comprehending how people's behavior impacts reputation and company performance. Risk is complex, global in nature and continues to challenge the most talented of business leaders. Unless risk is viewed in the round and the board has the relevant insight on its people, 2013 and beyond will continue to be mired with corporate scandals.

2013 In Review: Developed Economies Retake Growth-Engine Role

By Brenda Cronin 
885 words
31 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A7
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The global economy's jagged recovery inched forward in 2013, with fast-growing emerging markets losing pace while developed nations gained strength.

The 2013 performance reflected something of a role reversal among the players. After years of notching far slower growth than many emerging-market counterparts, some leading economies are at last showing strength. The global snapshot also reveals the far-flung effects of monetary policy, as decisions by central bankers in developed nations reverberated through emerging markets.

In 2013, central banks in the U.S., Japan and Europe showered money on their economies, held interest rates low and promised to continue to do so in a bid to animate a recovery that remains tepid almost five years after the worst recession since the Great Depression. In emerging markets such as Brazil and India, domestic demand softened and exports sagged as rates were boosted to stem inflation.

Despite the contrasting fortunes, the overall global economy appeared to be on a surer footing as the year ended. The International Monetary Fund forecasts that world output will grow 3.6% in 2014, compared with a 2.9% estimate for 2013.

The combination of fast-growing economies slowing slightly while the developed world picks up the pace sets the stage for "synchronized growth," in 2014, said Tu Packard, senior economist at Moody'sAnalytics. That means, she said, that "demand from developed markets will support developing-market growth, and they will support each other."

As the year unfolded, the euro zone limped out of recession. In Japan and the U.S., consumers repaired their personal balance sheets and resumed spending, although businesses remained wary about committing to new workers or investment.

Japan, several years late to the stimulus party, in April launched a bond-buying campaign that surpassed the scale of the Federal Reserve's $85 billion-a-month program as a share of gross domestic product. The European Central Bank cut its main interest rate to record territory, lowering it to 0.25% in November. The ECB said in December that the currency bloc's economy likely will have shrunk 0.4% this year before growing by 1.1% in 2014.

The U.S. illustrates one half of the shifting dynamic, with strength in the labor and housing markets as well as third-quarter growth at a 4.1% annualized pace. The coming year won't be entirely free of economic hurdles, cautioned Eric Lascelles, chief economist of RBC Global Asset Management, but the headwinds "will be distinctly lighter than last year."

The U.S. economy picked up momentum in 2013 as the drag from changes in government spending and taxation faded. Although cuts in government spending are expected to slightly diminish 2014's growth, they are likely to be a far less powerful brake than the payroll-tax increase, earlier spending cuts and fiscal brinkmanship that affected businesses and consumers in 2013. The two-year budget deal reached by Congress at year's end has defused some economic uncertainty as well.

The U.S. unemployment rate, at 7.0%, is well below its recession peak of 10% in October 2009, but above the 4.7% level before the downturn. More jobs, along with rising stock and housing markets, helped propel household net worth in the third quarter to a record level. However, the gains in wealth tilt heavily toward those invested in stocks, said Tom Porcelli, chief U.S. economist of RBC Capital Markets.

Against this generally improved backdrop, the Fed judged the U.S. economy healthy enough to receive less support and decided at its December meeting to cut back its bond-buying program.

Showing the fraught relationship between policy in developed markets and performance in emerging markets, the prospect of a Fed cutback in bond buying roiled financial markets in many developing countries earlier in the year. With the Fed keeping interest rates near zero for almost five years, investors have sought higher returns elsewhere, in part driving the boom in developing markets. When Fed monetary policy eventually returns to historically normal conditions, these global capital flows will tend to seek out the higher rates and greater stability offered by developed markets.

So far, the global economic recalibration is playing out in a relatively measured way in emerging markets despite the bumps.

China's economy expanded at the slowest rate in 20 years, crimping growth across the region. But the second half of the year brought reassurance that the world's second-largest economy had avoided a hard landing.

Decelerating growth, a weak currency and rising prices continued to bedevil India, where the central bank in October raised one of its key lending rates in hopes of slowing inflation. Aging infrastructure and a difficult climate for foreign investment weighed on Asia's third-largest economy, where the rupee was hit hard midyear by talk of the coming Fed reduction in bond buying.

In Latin America, many nations fell short of early-2013 growth forecasts. Brazil, the region's powerhouse, is expected to have expanded just over 2%, missing predictions of 4%-plus growth. With industry moribund, forecasts for 2014 -- when Brazil will host the soccer World Cup and hold presidential elections -- aren't optimistic.

Special
Realistic Financial Resolutions for 2014

1,526 words
26 December 2013
16:16
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Making financial resolutions for the new year is easy sticking to them is an entirely different story.

With this in mind, we posed the following question to The Experts: What's one realistic financial resolution people can make?

This discussion relates to the latest Wealth Management Report and formed the basis of a discussion on The Experts blog on Dec. 17.

OLIVIA S. MITCHELL: Each New Year's Eve, millions of us make financial resolutions for the next 12 months and predictably, most of us never end up keeping them. But as this year-end approaches, it's time to heed Benjamin Franklin, a very wise man, who knew a secret: "You may delay but time will not, and lost time is never found again."

So what should we look out for in 2014? Here are my suggestions:

Figure out how to reward yourself when you do something difficult. My colleague Katherine Milkman discovered that people who can only read their favorite trashy novels at the gym are much more likely to get their exercise. Dina Pomeranz shows us how to make not spending fun with a saving buddy you text/email every time you move toward your goal. Set up a low-cost online savings account with a motivating title ("My Reward Fund"), and put any change you have toward it. It'll add up.

Make it easy on yourself. Put every bill you can on autopay, including the bill for your own retirement. Call your human-resources office or log on to your retirement-savings website and bump up your pension contributions. Be sure to put in enough to get your full employer match for the best return on your saving dollar. If possible, set up auto-escalation so that every year, your percentage contribution to the retirement plan increases automatically. As Mother used to say, "If you don't see it, you won't spend it!"

Invest in financial literacy: It'll pay you the rest of your life. Our financial knowledge quiz is a nice way to take stock of what you need to learn, and some fun free videos can help you make better financial decisions. Wharton also has a free website for educators, students and just plain folks offering over 400 lessons on personal finance, economics, entrepreneurship, accounting and marketing.

Olivia S. Mitchell is a professor of business economics and public policy at the Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania where she focuses on pensions, household finance and risk management.

GREG MCBRIDE: Track your spending. By consistently tracking your spending every dollar that goes out the door this becomes the starting point for other good financial habits such as setting and adhering to a budget, regular savings and maximizing efficiency in your spending. The essence of building wealth requires someone to spend less than they bring in, and tracking your spending against a budget is the scorecard to which you can hold yourself accountable.

Greg McBride (@BankrateGreg) is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

MANISHA THAKOR: This new year, commit to paying your financial knowledge forward by sharing your best and worst financial moves with someone younger whom you care about.

Unlike other areas of our life fitness, nutrition, parenting, etc. our financial well-being isn't a topic that many of us talk about socially with our family and friends. As a result, many young people head out into their work lives with unrealistic expectations around spending and savings. Just think of the difference you could make in someone's lives by sharing lessons such as these:

Avoiding credit-card debt, and if you do have it, developing a plan to pay off more than the minimum monthly payment each month.

Starting to save for retirement (via 401(k)s, 403b, IRAs) as early as possible in your working years.

How to figure out how much car or home you can truly afford (Hint: It's usually less than a bank is willing to lend you!)

As an added benefit, when you share your hard-earned financial knowledge with the next generation you will naturally find yourself becoming more mindful about your own finances. It's the same way, once you start walking or going to the gym regularly, you naturally find yourself being more mindful of what you eat.

So this 2014, commit to helping a young person in your family, your office, or your community increase their financial well-being. That one simple step will pay dividends to you as well!

Manisha Thakor (@ManishaThakor) is founder and chief executive of Santa Fe, N.M.-based MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC.

LARRY ZIMPLEMAN: The most realistic and important financial resolution that people can make is to live within their means. That means setting aside some amounts to invest out of every paycheck not just when it's convenient. It's important to pay yourself just like you pay for food, transportation, housing, etc. You will gain a real feeling of control once you see those amounts accumulate over time.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

MIKE PIPER: Save 1% more. That is, increase your retirement savings contributions by 1% of your gross income.

Near the end of each year, financial planner and blogger Jim Blankenshiprallies the personal finance blogosphereto encourage people tosave 1% more per year. And it is a great idea.

Bumping up your savings rate by 1% results in a small change to your take-home pay so small that you might not even notice the difference in terms of your monthly budget. But the impact on your net worth over time could be dramatic.

As of this writing, the personal savings rate in the U.S. isjust 4.9%. In other words, if you're currently average, to increase your savings rate by 1%, the proportion by which you would have to reduce your spending is very small, but the proportion by which your savings rate would increase is huge (roughly one-fifth).

This strategy can work wonders if you're young. Increase your savings rate by 1% this year, then do the same thing next year. And the year after that. If you start right when you get your first job out of school, you'll be saving at a pretty good pace well before you hit the peak earning years of your career.

Mike Piper (@michaelrpiper) is a Missouri-licensed CPA and the author of the blog ObliviousInvestor.com. He is also the author of several personal-finance books, including his latest, "Social Security Made Simple."

CHARLES ROTBLUT: One resolution you can easily complete before January ends is to create an emergency plan for your finances. This is a document instructing your spouse, children or trusted adviser on what to do if you are suddenly unable to manage your finances. Ideally, this document should cover how you want your portfolio managed, where your banking and investment accounts are located, all outstanding loans, what bills are sent to you in electronic form or automatically paid, and insurance information. It doesn't need to include account numbers, but the person you trust to step in should be able to step in to either directly access those accounts or have power of attorney enabling them to do so.

Having an emergency plan does three things. First, it helps to ensure your finances are managed the way you would like them to be. Second, it creates one less worry for your spouse or children at a time when they will be stressed. Third, it gives you a blueprint to fall back upon when you are worried about what the market is doing and are unsure of what the best long-term decision is.

Charles Rotblut (@charlesrotblut) is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

MICHELLE PERRY HIGGINS:Instead of trying to completely change spending or savings habits, I say: Start small. For example, my "1% rule" is an easy resolution to make and keep. My recommendation to investors is to increase their retirement savings by a mere 1%. That is it. One small percentage increase. The amount isn't outrageous and is therefore achievable in most budgets. Many times I hear, "Michelle, I didn't even notice it was gone when I got my paycheck." But when they check their retirement savings account balance at the end of the year, they are often surprised at how much of a difference that 1% can make.

Michelle Perry Higgins (@RetirementMPH) is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

MATT HOUGAN: Save in the future. Many of us will get a raise in the new year. Set aside half of it to fund your retirement savings, create a rainy-day fund, or contribute to your kid's 529 plan.

Because it's money you don't have today, you probably won't even notice.

Matt Hougan (@Matt_Hougan) is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

Special
Aside from Fees, What's the Best Way to Assess Financial Advisers?

1,539 words
26 December 2013
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The price of a financial adviser can tell you only so much about his or her ability to manage your money.

With this issue in mind, we posed the following question to The Experts: Aside from fees, what's the best way for individuals to assess a potential financial adviser?

This discussion relates to the latest Wealth Management Report and formed the basis of a discussion on The Experts blog on Dec. 18.

LARRY ZIMPLEMAN: First of all, you should conduct interviews to find the right financial adviser, just like you'd pick a doctor, dentist or any other professional. Fees matter but other things are also important.

Find out what qualifications the adviser has (certified financial consultant, chartered financial analysts, etc.). Ask how many years they've been in the business and ask for references (and check them!). However, the most important thing to evaluate is whether the adviser's view of risk and reward is identical to yours. If you are risk averse, make sure they share your point of view. If you believe in long-term, asset-allocation strategies, make sure they are in sync with that.

Finally, once you choose a financial adviser, make sure you review the results regularly and that the adviser is carrying out your intended strategy.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group.

MIKE PIPER: Read the adviser's Form ADV Part 2. This is a form that advisers must file with the Securities and Exchange Commission or with the state in which they are registered. It tells you all sorts of information about the adviser, written in plain English, with very little sales talk. "Just the facts," so to speak.

You can learn everything about the adviser''s compensation: Do they charge an hourly rate? A percentage fee based on assets under management? Commissions? Are the fees negotiable?

You can learn about the adviser's investment philosophy to check whether it matches with your own: Are they a buy-hold-and-rebalance investor? Or do they believe in shifting asset allocation as a part of a market timing (aka dynamic asset allocation, aka tactical asset allocation) strategy?Do they prefer low-cost index funds and ETFs? Or do they use actively managed funds in the hope of achieving outperformance? Or do they use individual stocks and bonds?

You can check whether the adviser offers services that are a good fit for your needs: Do they provide comprehensive financial planning? Or do they focus exclusively on portfolio management?If they do provide financial-planning services, can you purchase such services on an as-needed basis, or do you have to sign up for a continuing fee?

Often, if you have several advisers you're considering, checking each of their ADV Part 2 brochures will be a quick way to rule out some of the advisers and develop questions to ask the others when interviewing them.

To get a hold of an adviser's Form ADV Part 2 brochure, you can contact them and request it or, if you would prefer not to give the adviser your contact information, you can look up the firm's form on theSEC Investment Adviser Search website.

Mike Piper (@michaelrpiper) is a Missouri-licensed CPA and the author of the blog ObliviousInvestor.com. He is also the author of several personal-finance books, including his latest, "Social Security Made Simple."

CHARLES ROTBLUT: Beyond doing a background check (contact your state regulators, do a search on brokercheck.finra.org and adviserinfo.sec.gov, confirm credentials with the certifying organization, and then do a Google search on the adviser's name to see what comes up), talk to potential advisers about their management style. What type of investments do they personally favor? Do they like to be more aggressive or more conservative? How often can you expect to hear from them? Do they send out regular emails or newsletters? If you call with a question, will you realistically get to speak to them or will you be directed to assistants instead? How quickly can you expect a response under normal circumstances?

The greatest adviser in the world can be unsuitable for you if his or her personality and yours don't match. Every adviser has a certain approach to working with clients and every investor has different needs. This is why it is important to have a very open and candid discussion with an adviser you are considering. Talk about how often you want or don't want to be contacted. Discuss your investment style and whether you want to be aggressive or conservative. Bring up any investments you want to manage separately from the adviser and how much or how little input you want on it. Be very clear about what you want in the relationship and clearly state that you will fire an adviser whose approach differs from what he or she tells you.

It's better for both you and the adviser to identify differences in style before a formal relationship is established. A good adviser will be willing to give you the answers you don't want to hear. A bad adviser will smile and tell you what you want to hear even when he or she knows it isn't true. If you have doubts that the adviser is being sincere in answering your questions, walk away and find someone else.

Charles Rotblut (@charlesrotblut) is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors.

MANISHA THAKOR: To me, the greatest value that a financial adviser provides is helping you avoid costly missteps. The most effective way to do this is through the use of a clearly articulated written financial plan. Now, I'm not talking about an 80-page printout in a three-ring binder from some off-the-shelf financial software. I'm talking about a truly customized, short and actionable financial road map that you can refer to when making key financial decisions and/or when the markets are choppy. As such, I think the best way to assess a potential financial adviser is whether or not they provide this. (I am assuming you've already established that the adviser uses a fair and transparent fee structure and operates as a fiduciary versus under the suitability standard).

As an example of what I'm talking about, for each of my clients, I create a one-page summary where we visually depict where a client currently stands financially, where they ultimately want to go, and what specific action steps they need to take to get there. This document is supported by a Monte Carlo analysis to identify optimal savings rates if in the asset-accumulation stage or spending rates if in the asset drawdown stage and a written Investment Policy Statement clearly articulating an asset allocation and cash management strategy.

The benefit of an adviser who works in this manner is threefold:

This type of customized planning requires an adviser get to know you deeply (your goals, values, dreams, fears)

The presence of a written plan ensures you have a system of checks and balances for when your emotions might come into play (think: Did you blink in 2007-2009?)

This process moves the emphasis from trying to beat the market (which study after study shows is a loser's game) to truly meeting your life goals.

Manisha Thakor (@ManishaThakor) is founder and chief executive of Santa Fe, N.M.-based MoneyZen Wealth Management LLC.

TOM BRAKKE: It is important to find out where an adviser is on the spectrum between a "relationship person" and an "investment person." Doubtless they have a combination of those skills, but few have the two attributes in approximate balance. Usually advisers for individuals skew toward the relationship end of the scale.

Understanding the capabilities of a prospective adviser will help you determine whether you are a match in terms of communications. But it will also let you see what expertise others in the adviser's organization will need to provide to help you reach your goals.

It is as frustrating dealing with an "investment person" who doesn't understand your relationship needs as it is working with a "relationship person" who doesn't have a depth of knowledge on investment matters. Therefore, it's important to find the right fit and to not be afraid to get a second opinion every once in a while.

(For more on this topic, see the article I wrote for the CFA Institute in 2012.)

Tom Brakke, CFA (@researchpuzzler) is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

MATT HOUGAN: Make sure they have a fiduciary duty to act in your best interests. Then, ask them what they think about index funds. They don't have to be index-fund zealots, but if they think that active management still works in all areas of the markets, they are selling you a pile of goods.

Matt Hougan (@Matt_Hougan) is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

pict101.png

Special
The Experts' Biggest Worries About the Markets in 2014

1,860 words
24 December 2013
13:56
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

With 2013 nearly behind us now and the new year just around the corner, we decided to ask The Experts the following question: What's your biggest worry about the markets in 2014?

This discussion relates to the latest Investing in Funds & ETFs Report and formed the basis of a discussion on The Experts blog on Dec. 6.

GUS SAUTER : I'm not worried about the markets in 2014. They may go up or they may go down. If they go up, we make money. If they go down, we can invest new money at lower prices. I can hardly wait for whatever happens.

George U. "Gus" Sauter is a senior consultant to Vanguard Group Inc . From 2003 through 2012, Mr. Sauter served as Vanguard's chief investment officer.

SHARYL GARRETT: In the U.S., we are currently undergoing a serious leadership crisis over a number of critical subjects: health care, re-regulations of financial advisers, employment, climate change, you name it. Colleagues have labeled this "Congress Risk."With so many American investors potentially or directly impacted by these issues, I feel that the work Congress gets done, or fails to get done, will have a major impact on U.S. investors' attitudes about investing in the very short term.

Another challenging area is finding reasonable investing opportunities in the fixed-income markets with our interest rates being so low.Some investors are taking on substantially more risk than they have traditionally to receive a similar rate of return.If you've got a home mortgage or any debt, for that matter and you've got fixed-income investments, consider paying off the debt instead.You will not get that high of an after-tax return from such a secure investment in 2014.

Longer term, I'm worried about the global financial markets and the weakening of the U.S. dollar.However, my guess is that nothing much will change along these lines in the next year or so.

Sheryl Garrett (@SherylGarrett) is founder of the Garrett Planning Network Inc.

LARRY ZIMPLEMAN : I think the biggest consensus worry for the markets in 2014 is the possible rise in interest rates causing fixed-income funds to post negative returns.

If that is a concern for you, my suggestion is that you: 1) shorten your fixed-income duration, and 2) reduce your fixed-income exposure to the lower bound of your longer term asset allocation but do not pull completely out of fixed-income investments. Market performance is random enough that you still want to maintain some exposure despite the risk of rising interest rates.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group .

RICK FERRI: My biggest worry is that investors worry too much about 2014. Don't worry about the return of any asset class over any single year. Establish a portfolio based on your needs and hold it for the long term.

My second worry is that investors are still paying too much for investment products and advice. There is no reason to pay a lot of money for access to the financial markets. There are many excellent low-cost index funds and ETFs available today. In addition, the era of the 1% adviser fee is over.

Rick Ferri is founder of Portfolio Solutions LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is RickFerri.com.

CHARLES ROTBLUT : The immediate worry is yet another fiscal standoff in January and February. Everyone knows the potential for this occurring, but I won't predict what politicians will or won't do.

The other identifiable risk is economic growth. Does growth accelerate next year, does it stay steady or does it slow? Nobody knows, but a pausing of economic expansion would hurt profit growth and call prevailing stock valuations in to question.

The biggest risk to investors is not what will or won't happen next year, but rather inflation and longevity risk (the risk of outliving your savings). These are long-term risks that are best combated by staying invested and periodically adjusting your allocations back to target.

It is human nature to focus on what might happen over the short term, but it is more important to think long term.

Charles Rotblut (@charlesrotblut) is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors .

GEORGE PAPADOPOULOS: We have had five straight years of positive stock-market returns with no meaningful correction, which is unusual. At some point, the inevitable correction will come; this is how the markets roll.

I am more worried about what the politicians in Washington may do to harm our steady economic progress and turnaround. The level of partisanship has reached deafening levels, and the government shutdown was simply inexcusable. I am also sad to see that no meaningful tax reform will likely happen in 2014.

George Papadopoulos is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families. You can follow him at twitter (@feeonlyplanner), connect with him at Google+ or visit his firm's website.

TOM BRAKKE: There is an active and healthy debate going on about the valuation of the stock market. Some measures show it as quite overvalued and some put it into above-normal valuation. It is certainly not "cheap" on an historical basis.

If you think of valuation as an odds board, the odds of strong future stock-market returns have been getting worse for investors as prices have risen without very much support in the way of better sales and earnings. And pessimists would argue that with margins at historically high levels and interest rates relatively low, the surprises would be negative ones rather than positive ones.

But no formula can capture the calculus of the market in the short term, so the historical odds may not matter during 2014. There is no way to know for sure.

Tom Brakke, CFA (@researchpuzzler) is a consultant, writer and investment adviser who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

ELEANOR BLAYNEY : I worry about the continued growth of financial assets in 2014 or, more specifically, the way this growth is being achieved.

The Federal Reserve's ongoing quantitative easing has had the effect of inflating asset values of publicly traded businesses. Without growth in revenues, companies have nevertheless seen their valuations increase, as a result of Fed-engineered low interest rates used to discount the future earnings of these enterprises. The stock-market prognosticators seem to wade deeper into the Kool Aid as the market bounds higher, pointing to "still reasonable" price/earnings ratios which, they say, are a sign that the bull will keep running.

It strikes me as out of whack when every piece of genuinely good news more jobs, industrial production, retail sales is an occasion for a stock market nose dive, with traders panicked that QE will come to an end. In my book, positive numbers are something to invest into, rather than run from.

I worry about the irrationality of knowing full well that interest rates can't get any lower, but behaving as if they could.

As an advocate for consumers, it also bothers me that the wealth and income gap in this country is getting wider and more politically and socially disruptive. Low interest rates have benefited only the wealthiest in this country. For those who desperately need credit, quantitative easing might as well be taking place on another planet. Credit-card rates and payday loans are still at usurious levels.

But, all said and done, I wonder if I should be less worried than, in fact, I am. Twenty-five years as a CFP professional has taught me that worry is a short-term indulgence.It's fun, but not necessarily wise, to gripe about what's wrong with the world today. Good planning and a long-term perspective works far better than Valium.

Eleanor Blayney (@EleanorBlayney) is consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards.

MICHELLE PERRY HIGGINS: Contrary to what you might think, I don't worry about the stock market. Perhaps that comes with having been in my profession for more than 17 years and seeing the good, bad and downright ugly when it comes to investment cycles.

One of the things that concern me most in general is that I see so many investors that still fail to see the value in creating a long-term financial plan. Another concern I have is how unprepared most people are for the unexpected. It is always a good idea to have a solid written plan in place in the event of illness, an incapacitating accident or even death. That's what will worry me in 2014 and until the day I retire.

Michelle Perry Higgins (@RetirementMPH) is a financial planner and principal at California Financial Advisors.

JOHN ROGERS : While 2013 has been a strong year for most investors, I would sound a note of caution for 2014 due to market fragility. Several surveys, including one the CFA Institute ran recently with Edelman, show that investors remain wary of financial markets and market practices. In addition, we sponsored a survey just released by the Economist Intelligence Unit which found that although financial-services executives overwhelmingly recognize the importance of ethical behavior in the industry, there is still a significant gap between that belief and the industry's practices.

The "risk off" trade, which sees investors exiting stocks and similar assets en masse, could be triggered by a wide range of events. With trust in the markets and institutions at a low ebb, the trigger finger is quite twitchy.

John Rogers is the president and chief executive of the CFA Institute.

GREG MCBRIDE : Let's start with what I'm not worried about. I'm not worried about a stock-market correction. Rather, I expect it, and will welcome it as a buying opportunity.

My biggest worry is that the stock market will continue to rise uninterrupted at a pace much faster than the underlying fundamentals such as revenue and earnings growth warrant. That would only bring greater pain later when investors refocus on the fundamentals.

Greg McBride (@BankrateGreg) is a senior financial analyst and vice president for Bankrate.com, providing analysis and advice on personal finance.

MATT HOUGAN: Crowding. Investors have rushed into high-yielding corners of the market like emerging-markets debt, master limited partnerships (MLPs), bank loans and other strategies. It makes sense: Treasurys yield next to nothing, and investors need income. Buying something like bank loans which offer relatively high yields and no interest rate risk is a no-brainer.

But we've all seen this story before. When something's a no-brainer in investing, it's ends badly.

Ask yourself: When is the last time that investors poured billions of dollars into an asset that most of us have never heard of before &and it turned out well?

Matt Hougan (@Matt_Hougan) is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.
